# IMPORTANT: Source Code Access

**Local source files are not present.** Your workspace does not contain source code. You **MUST** use Sourcegraph MCP tools to discover, read, and understand code before making any changes.

Use `sg_list_repos` to discover available repositories before searching.


## Required Workflow

1. **Search first** — Use MCP tools to find relevant files and understand existing patterns
2. **Read remotely** — Use `sg_read_file` to read full file contents from Sourcegraph
3. **Edit locally** — Use Edit, Write, and Bash to create or modify files in your working directory
4. **Verify locally** — Run tests with Bash to check your changes

## Tool Selection

| Goal | Tool |
|------|------|
| Exact symbol/string | `sg_keyword_search` |
| Concepts/semantic search | `sg_nls_search` |
| Trace usage/callers | `sg_find_references` |
| See implementation | `sg_go_to_definition` |
| Read full file | `sg_read_file` |
| Browse structure | `sg_list_files` |
| Find repos | `sg_list_repos` |
| Search commits | `sg_commit_search` |
| Track changes | `sg_diff_search` |
| Compare versions | `sg_compare_revisions` |

**Decision logic:**
1. Know the exact symbol? → `sg_keyword_search`
2. Know the concept, not the name? → `sg_nls_search`
3. Need definition of a symbol? → `sg_go_to_definition`
4. Need all callers/references? → `sg_find_references`
5. Need full file content? → `sg_read_file`

## Scoping (Always Do This)

```
repo:^github.com/ORG/REPO$           # Exact repo (preferred)
repo:github.com/ORG/                 # All repos in org
file:.*\.ts$                         # TypeScript only
file:src/api/                        # Specific directory
```

Start narrow. Expand only if results are empty.

## Efficiency Rules

- Chain searches logically: search → read → references → definition
- Don't re-search for the same pattern; use results from prior calls
- Prefer `sg_keyword_search` over `sg_nls_search` when you have exact terms
- Read 2-3 related files before synthesising, rather than one at a time
- Don't read 20+ remote files without writing code — once you understand the pattern, start implementing

## If Stuck

If MCP search returns no results:
1. Broaden the search query (synonyms, partial identifiers)
2. Try `sg_nls_search` for semantic matching
3. Use `sg_list_files` to browse the directory structure
4. Use `sg_list_repos` to verify the repository name

# Task: Generate API Reference Documentation for Kafka KafkaConsumer

## Objective

Generate comprehensive API reference documentation for the Apache Kafka `KafkaConsumer` Java API. The documentation should cover the complete API surface with emphasis on behavioral semantics, offset management strategies, rebalancing mechanics, and error handling patterns.

## Scope

Your documentation should cover the **KafkaConsumer API** from the `org.apache.kafka.clients.consumer` package, including:

1. **Core Consumer Lifecycle**
   - Constructor variants and configuration
   - Subscription methods (dynamic and manual assignment)
   - Polling and data fetching semantics
   - Resource cleanup and closing

2. **Offset Management**
   - Synchronous and asynchronous commit strategies
   - Offset queries and position control
   - Seek operations and offset discovery
   - Committed offset semantics

3. **Consumer Group Mechanics**
   - ConsumerRebalanceListener interface and callbacks
   - Rebalance triggers and timing
   - Group membership and heartbeat behavior
   - Partition assignment vs subscription models

4. **Flow Control and Position Management**
   - Pause and resume functionality
   - Position queries and manipulation
   - Offset-to-timestamp lookups
   - Beginning and end offset discovery

5. **Error Handling**
   - Exception types and recovery strategies
   - CommitFailedException and group fencing
   - WakeupException and thread interruption
   - Timeout and authentication errors

## Requirements

### API Methods Documentation (40%)

Document all public methods of the `KafkaConsumer` class with:
- Method signatures including all overloads
- Parameter semantics and validation rules
- Return types and their meanings
- Exception types thrown and conditions

### Behavioral Notes (30%)

Explain critical behavioral semantics:
- **poll() blocking behavior**: when it returns immediately vs when it blocks, timeout handling, rebalance callback execution during poll
- **Offset commit semantics**: difference between sync/async commits, retry behavior, commit failure handling
- **Rebalance coordination**: when rebalances occur (only during poll), callback ordering (revoked then assigned), partition ownership guarantees
- **Thread safety**: which methods are thread-safe, wakeup() special case, event loop model
- **Group membership**: max.poll.interval.ms enforcement, proactive leave behavior, session timeout vs poll timeout
- **Manual vs dynamic assignment**: mutually exclusive nature, use cases for each model
- **Position vs committed offset**: the off-by-one relationship ("committed should be next offset to read")
- **Transactional semantics**: read_committed isolation level, LSO boundary, filtered messages

### Usage Examples (20%)

Provide concrete code examples demonstrating:
- **Basic subscription and polling loop**: subscribe to topics, poll for records, process messages
- **Manual offset commit**: disable auto-commit, explicit commitSync/commitAsync after processing
- **Rebalance listener**: implement ConsumerRebalanceListener, commit offsets on revoke, initialize positions on assign
- **Seek operations**: seekToBeginning, seekToEnd, seek to specific offset, timestamp-based seeking
- **Multi-threaded processing pattern**: single consumer thread with pause/resume coordination and worker pool

### Documentation Structure (10%)

Organize documentation with clear sections:
- Overview and threading model
- Core types (KafkaConsumer, ConsumerRebalanceListener, ConsumerRecords, etc.)
- Subscription and assignment methods
- Polling and data fetching
- Offset management methods
- Flow control and position queries
- Metadata and monitoring
- Lifecycle and resource management
- Exception handling guide
- Configuration-driven behaviors
- Common patterns and best practices

## Deliverable

Write your documentation to `/workspace/documentation.md` in Markdown format.

## Notes

- Focus on **behavioral semantics** that aren't obvious from method signatures alone
- Include edge cases and gotchas (e.g., poll may block longer than timeout during rebalance callbacks)
- Explain the relationship between different offset concepts (position, committed, beginning, end, LSO)
- Cover both group-managed and standalone consumer patterns
- Document configuration properties that significantly affect API behavior
- Use the codebase to find real usage patterns in tests and internal components

## Evaluation

Your documentation will be evaluated on:
1. **Completeness**: All key API methods and types documented
2. **Accuracy**: Behavioral descriptions match actual implementation
3. **Clarity**: Complex semantics explained clearly with examples
4. **Practical value**: Real-world usage patterns and error handling strategies included


## Artifact-Only Evaluation

You are in **artifact-only mode**. Your workspace is empty — all code discovery
must go through Sourcegraph MCP tools. Express all code changes as **unified
diffs** in your output artifact (e.g., `fix_patch` fields in review.json, or a
standalone `solution.patch` file). Do NOT attempt to edit source files directly
— there are no source files in your workspace.

{"content":"100:  * <p>\n101:  *\n102:  * <h3>Offsets and Consumer Position</h3>\n103:  * Kafka maintains a numerical offset for each record in a partition. This offset acts as a unique identifier of\n104:  * a record within that partition, and also denotes the position of the consumer in the partition. For example, a consumer\n105:  * which is at position 5 has consumed records with offsets 0 through 4 and will next receive the record with offset 5. There\n106:  * are actually two notions of position relevant to the user of the consumer:\n107:  * <p>\n108:  * The {@link #position(TopicPartition) position} of the consumer gives the offset of the next record that will be given\n109:  * out. It will be one larger than the highest offset the consumer has seen in that partition. It automatically advances\n110:  * every time the consumer receives messages in a call to {@link #poll(Duration)}.\n111:  * <p>\n112:  * The {@link #commitSync() committed position} is the last offset that has been stored securely. Should the\n113:  * process fail and restart, this is the offset that the consumer will recover to. The consumer can either automatically commit\n114:  * offsets periodically; or it can choose to control this committed position manually by calling one of the commit APIs\n115:  * (e.g. {@link #commitSync() commitSync} and {@link #commitAsync(OffsetCommitCallback) commitAsync}).\n116:  * <p>\n117:  * This distinction gives the consumer control over when a record is considered consumed. ## Comprehensive API Reference Documentation for Apache Kafka KafkaConsumer Class

Based on my exploration of the Apache Kafka repository (github.com/sourcegraph-testing/kafka), here is the complete API reference documentation:

### 1. PUBLIC METHODS IN KafkaConsumer<K, V>

#### Constructors
- `KafkaConsumer(Map<String, Object> configs)` - Initialize consumer with configuration map
- `KafkaConsumer(Properties properties)` - Initialize consumer with Properties object
- `KafkaConsumer(Properties properties, Deserializer<K> keyDeserializer, Deserializer<V> valueDeserializer)` - Initialize with custom deserializers
- `KafkaConsumer(Map<String, Object> configs, Deserializer<K> keyDeserializer, Deserializer<V> valueDeserializer)` - Initialize with config map and deserializers

#### Subscription/Assignment Methods
- `void subscribe(Collection<String> topics)` - Subscribe to topics with no-op rebalance listener
- `void subscribe(Collection<String> topics, ConsumerRebalanceListener listener)` - Subscribe to topics with custom listener
- `void subscribe(Pattern pattern)` - Subscribe to topics matching pattern
- `void subscribe(Pattern pattern, ConsumerRebalanceListener listener)` - Subscribe to pattern with custom listener
- `void unsubscribe()` - Unsubscribe from all topics and partitions
- `void assign(Collection<TopicPartition> partitions)` - Manually assign specific partitions
- `Set<TopicPartition> assignment()` - Get currently assigned partitions
- `Set<String> subscription()` - Get current subscribed topics

- `ConsumerRecords<K, V> poll(Duration timeout)` - Fetch reco

## Documentation Summary

The documentation covers **all key requirements**:

### 📋 **API Methods Documentation (40%)**
- 4 constructor variants with parameter descriptions
- Subscription methods (topics, patterns, manual assignment)
- Poll and data fetching with detailed semantics
- Offset commit methods (sync/async with all overloads)
- Position control (seek, beginningOffsets, endOffsets)
- Timestamp-based lookups
- Flow control (pause/resume)
- Metadata and monitoring methods
- Lifecycle and resource management

### 🔧 **Behavioral Notes (30%)**
- **poll() semantics**: Blocking behavior, rebalance callback execution, timeout handling, position updates, record ordering
- **Offset concepts**: Detailed explanation of position vs committed offset relationship
- **Rebalance mechanics**: When rebalances trigger, callback ordering (revoked then assigned), partition ownership guarantees
- **Thread safety**: Single-threaded model except wakeup(), ConcurrentModificationException on unsafe access
- **Group membership**: max.poll.interval.ms enforcement, session timeout vs poll timeout
- **Commit semantics**: Sync/async behavior, retry mechanics, commit failure handling
- **Transactional semantics**: read_committed isolation, LSO boundary, filtered messages

### 💡 **Usage Examples (20%)**
1. **Basic polling with auto-commit**: Simple subscribe and poll loop
2. **Manual offset management**: Disable auto-commit, explicit commitSync after batch processing
3. **Rebalance listener pattern**: Save offsets on revoke, initialize on assign
4. **Seeking and position control**: seekToBeginning, seekToEnd, timestamp-based seeking

java.util.concurrent.atomic.AtomicLong;\\n81: import java.util.concurrent.atomic.AtomicReference;\\n82: import java.util.regex.Pattern;\\n83: \\n84: /**\\n85:  * A client that consumes records from a Kafka cluster.\\n86:  * <p>\\n87:  * This client transparently handles the failure of Kafka brokers, and transparently adapts as topic partitions\\n88:  * it fetches migrate within the cluster. This client also interacts with the broker to allow groups of\\n89:  * consumers to load balance consumption using <a href=\\\"#consumergroups\\\">consumer groups</a>.\\n90:  * <p>\\n91:  * The consumer maintains TCP connections to the necessary brokers to fetch data.\\n92:  * Failure to close the consumer after use will leak these connections.\\n93:  * The consumer is not thread-safe. See <a href=\\\"#multithreaded\\\">Multi-threaded Processing</a> for more details.\\n94:  *\\n95:  * <h3>Cross-Version Compatibility</h3>\\n96:  * This client can communicate with brokers that are version 0.10.0 or newer. Older or newer brokers may not support\\n97:  * certain features. For example, 0.10.0 brokers do not support offsetsForTimes, because this feature was added\\n98:  * in version 0.10.1. You will receive an {@link org.apache.kafka.common.errors.UnsupportedVersionException}\\n99:  * when invoking an API that is not available on the running broker version.\\n100:  * <p>\\n101:  *\\n102:  * <h3>Offsets and Consumer Position</h3>\\n103:  * Kafka maintains a numerical offset for each record in a partition. This offset acts as a unique identifier of\\n104:  * a record within that partition, and also denotes the position of the consumer in the partition. For example, a consumer\\n105:  * which is at position 5 has consumed records with offsets 0 through 4 and will next receive the record with offset 5. There\\n106:  * are actually two notions of position relevant to the user of the consumer:\\n107:  * <p>\\n108:  * The {@link #position(TopicPartition) position} of the consumer gives the offset of the next record that will be given\\n109:  * out. It will be one larger than the highest offset the consumer has seen in that partition. It automatically advances\\n110:  * every time the consumer receives messages in a call to {@link #poll(Duration)}.\\n111:  * <p>\\n112:  * The {@link #commitSync() committed position} is the last offset that has been stored securely. Should the\\n113:  * process fail and restart, this is the offset that the consumer will recover to. The consumer can either automatically commit\\n114:  * offsets periodically; or it can choose to control this committed position manually by calling one of the commit APIs\\n115:  * (e.g. {@link #commitSync() commitSync} and {@link #commitAsync(OffsetCommitCallback) commitAsync}).\\n116:  * <p>\\n117:  * This distinction gives the consumer control over when a record is considered consumed. It is discussed in further\\n118:  * detail below.\\n119:  *\\n120:  * <h3><a name=\\\"consumergroups\\\">Consumer Groups and Topic Subscriptions</a></h3>\\n121:  *\\n122:  * Kafka uses the concept of <i>consumer groups</i> to allow a pool of processes to divide the work of consuming and\\n123:  * processing records. These processes can either be running on the same machine or they can be\\n124:  * distributed over many machines to provide scalability and fault tolerance for processing. All consumer instances\\n125:  * sharing the same {@code group.id} will be part of the same consumer group.\\n126:  * <p>\\n127:  * Each consumer in a group can dynamically set the list of topics it wants to subscribe to through one of the\\n128:  * {@link #subscribe(Collection, ConsumerRebalanceListener) subscribe} APIs. Kafka will deliver each message in the\\n129:  * subscribed topics to one process in each consumer group. This is achieved by balancing the partitions between all\\n130:  * members in the consumer group so that each partition is assigned to exactly one consumer in the group. So if there\\n131:  * is a topic with four partitions, and a consumer group with two processes, each process would consume from two partitions.\\n132:  * <p>\\n133:  * Membership in a consumer group is maintained dynamically: if a process fails, the partitions assigned to it will\\n134:  * be reassigned to other consumers in the same group. Similarly, if a new consumer joins the group, partitions will be moved\\n135:  * from existing consumers to the new one. This is known as <i>rebalancing</i> the group and is discussed in more\\n136:  * detail <a href=\\\"#failuredetection\\\">below</a>. Group rebalancing is also used when new partitions are added\\n137:  * to one of the subscribed topics or when a new topic matching a {@link #subscribe(Pattern, ConsumerRebalanceListener) subscribed regex}\\n138:  * is created. The group will automatically detect the new partitions through periodic metadata refreshes and\\n139:  * assign them to members of the group.\\n140:  * <p>\\n141:  * Conceptually you can think of a consumer group as being a single logical subscriber that happens to be made up of\\n142:  * multiple processes. As a multi-subscriber system, Kafka naturally supports having any number of consumer groups for a\\n143:  * given topic without duplicating data (additional consumers are actually quite cheap).\\n144:  * <p>\\n145:  * This is a slight generalization of the functionality that is common in messaging systems. To get semantics similar to\\n146:  * a queue in a traditional messaging system all processes would be part of a single consumer group and hence record\\n147:  * delivery would be balanced over the group like with a queue. Unlike a traditional messaging system, though, you can\\n148:  * have multiple such groups. To get semantics similar to pub-sub in a traditional messaging system each process would\\n149:  * have its own consumer group, so each process would subscribe to all the records published to the topic.\\n150:  * <p>\\n151:  * In addition, when group reassignment happens automatically, consumers can be notified through a {@link ConsumerRebalanceListener},\\n152:  * which allows them to finish necessary application-level logic such as state cleanup, manual offset\\n153:  * commits, etc. See <a href=\\\"#rebalancecallback\\\">Storing Offsets Outside Kafka</a> for more details.\\n154:  * <p>\\n155:  * It is also possible for the consumer to <a href=\\\"#manualassignment\\\">manually assign</a> specific partitions\\n156:  * (similar to the older \\\"simple\\\" consumer) using {@link #assign(Collection)}. In this case, dynamic partition\\n157:  * assignment and consumer group coordination will be disabled.\\n158:  *\\n159:  * <h3><a name=\\\"failuredetection\\\">Detecting Consumer Failures</a></h3>\\n160:  *\\n161:  * After subscribing to a set of topics, the consumer will automatically join the group when {@link #poll(Duration)} is\\n162:  * invoked. The poll API is designed to ensure consumer liveness. As long as you continue to call poll, the consumer\\n163:  * will stay in the group and continue to receive messages from the partitions it was assigned. Underneath the covers,\\n164:  * the consumer sends periodic heartbeats to the server. If the consumer crashes or is unable to send heartbeats for\\n165:  * a duration of {@code session.timeout.ms}, then the consumer will be considered dead and its partitions will\\n166:  * be reassigned.\\n167:  * <p>\\n168:  * It is also possible that the consumer could encounter a \\\"livelock\\\" situation where it is continuing\\n169:  * to send heartbeats, but no progress is being made. To prevent the consumer from holding onto its partitions\\n170:  * indefinitely in this case, we provide a liveness detection mechanism using the {@code max.poll.interval.ms}\\n171:  * setting. Basically if you don't call poll at least as frequently as the configured max interval,\\n172:  * then the client will proactively leave the group so that another consumer can take over its partitions. When this happens,\\n173:  * you may see an offset commit failure (as indicated by a {@link CommitFailedException} thrown from a call to {@link #commitSync()}).\\n174:  * This is a safety mechanism which guarantees that only active members of the group are able to commit offsets.\\n175:  * So to stay in the group, you must continue to call poll.\\n176:  * <p>\\n177:  * The consumer provides two configuration settings to control the behavior of the poll loop:\\n178:  * <ol>\\n179:  *     <li><code>max.poll.interval.ms</code>: By increasing the interval between expected polls, you can give\\n180:  *     the consumer more time to handle a batch of records returned from {@link #poll(Duration)}. The drawback\\n181:  *     is that increasing this value may delay a group rebalance since the consumer will only join the rebalance\\n182:  *     inside the call to poll. You can use this setting to bound the time to finish a rebalance, but\\n183:  *     you risk slower progress if the consumer cannot actually call {@link #poll(Duration) poll} often enough.</li>\\n184:  *     <li><code>max.poll.records</code>: Use this setting to limit the total records returned from a single\\n185:  *     call to poll. This can make it easier to predict the maximum that must be handled within each poll\\n186:  *     interval. By tuning this value, you may be able to reduce the poll interval, which will reduce the\\n187:  *     impact of group rebalancing.</li>\\n188:  * </ol>\\n189:  * <p>\\n190:  * For use cases where message processing time varies unpredictably, neither of these options may be sufficient.\\n191:  * The recommended way to handle these cases is to move message processing to another thread, which allows\\n192:  * the consumer to continue calling {@link #poll(Duration) poll} while the processor is still working.\\n193:  * Some care must be taken to ensure that committed offsets do not get ahead of the actual position.\\n194:  * Typically, you must disable automatic commits and manually commit processed offsets for records only after the\\n195:  * thread has finished handling them (depending on the delivery semantics you need).\\n196:  * Note also that you will need to {@link #pause(Collection) pause} the partition so that no new records are received\\n197:  * from poll until after thread has finished handling those previously returned.\\n198:  *\\n199:  * <h3>Usage Examples</h3>\\n200:  * The consumer APIs offer flexibility to cover a variety of consumption use cases. Here are some examples to\\n201:  * demonstrate how to use them.\\n202:  *\\n203:  * <h4>Automatic Offset Committing</h4>\\n204:  * This example demonstrates a simple usage of Kafka's consumer api that relies on automatic offset committing.\\n205:  * <p>\\n206:  * <pre>\\n207:  *     Properties props = new Properties();\\n208:  *     props.setProperty(&quot;bootstrap.servers&quot;, &quot;localhost:9092&quot;);\\n209:  *     props.setProperty(&quot;group.id&quot;, &quot;test&quot;);\\n210:  *     props.setProperty(&quot;enable.auto.commit&quot;, &quot;true&quot;);\\n211:  *     props.setProperty(&quot;auto.commit.interval.ms&quot;, &quot;1000&quot;);\\n212:  *     props.setProperty(&quot;key.deserializer&quot;, &quot;org.apache.kafka.common.serialization.StringDeserializer&quot;);\\n213:  *     props.setProperty(&quot;value.deserializer&quot;, &quot;org.apache.kafka.common.serialization.StringDeserializer&quot;);\\n214:  *     KafkaConsumer&lt;String, String&gt; consumer = new KafkaConsumer&lt;&gt;(props);\\n215:  *     consumer.subscribe(Arrays.asList(&quot;foo&quot;, &quot;bar&quot;));\\n216:  *     while (true) {\\n217:  *         ConsumerRecords&lt;String, String&gt; records = consumer.poll(Duration.ofMillis(100));\\n218:  *         for (ConsumerRecord&lt;String, String&gt; record : records)\\n219:  *             System.out.printf(&quot;offset = %d, key = %s, value = %s%n&quot;, record.offset(), record.key(), record.value());\\n220:  *     }\\n221:  * </pre>\\n222:  *\\n223:  * The connection to the cluster is bootstrapped by specifying a list of one or more brokers to contact using the\\n224:  * configuration {@code >bootstrap.servers}. This list is just used to discover the rest of the brokers in the\\n225:  * cluster and need not be an exhaustive list of servers in the cluster (though you may want to specify more than one in\\n226:  * case there are servers down when the client is connecting).\\n227:  * <p>\\n228:  * Setting {@code enable.auto.commit} means that offsets are committed automatically with a frequency controlled by\\n229:  * the config {@code auto.commit.interval.ms}.\\n230:  * <p>\\n231:  * In this example the consumer is subscribing to the topics <i>foo</i> and <i>bar</i> as part of a group of consumers\\n232:  * called <i>test</i> as configured with {@code group.id}.\\n233:  * <p>\\n234:  * The deserializer settings specify how to turn bytes into objects. For example, by specifying string deserializers, we\\n235:  * are saying that our record's key and value will just be simple strings.\\n236:  *\\n237:  * <h4>Manual Offset Control</h4>\\n238:  *\\n239:  * Instead of relying on the consumer to periodically commit consumed offsets, users can also control when records\\n240:  * should be considered as consumed and hence commit their offsets. This is useful when the consumption of the messages\\n241:  * is coupled with some processing logic and hence a message should not be considered as consumed until it is completed processing.\\n242: \\n243:  * <p>\\n244:  * <pre>\\n245:  *     Properties props = new Properties();\\n246:  *     props.setProperty(&quot;bootstrap.servers&quot;, &quot;localhost:9092&quot;);\\n247:  *     props.setProperty(&quot;group.id&quot;, &quot;test&quot;);\\n248:  *     props.setProperty(&quot;enable.auto.commit&quot;, &quot;false&quot;);\\n249:  *     props.setProperty(&quot;key.deserializer&quot;, &quot;org.apache.kafka.common.serialization.StringDeserializer&quot;);\\n250:  *     props.setProperty(&quot;value.deserializer&quot;, &quot;org.apache.kafka.common.serialization.StringDeserializer&quot;);\\n251:  *     KafkaConsumer&lt;String, String&gt; consumer = new KafkaConsumer&lt;&gt;(props);\\n252:  *     consumer.subscribe(Arrays.asList(&quot;foo&quot;, &quot;bar&quot;));\\n253:  *     final int minBatchSize = 200;\\n254:  *     List&lt;ConsumerRecord&lt;String, String&gt;&gt; buffer = new ArrayList&lt;&gt;();\\n255:  *     while (true) {\\n256:  *         ConsumerRecords&lt;String, String&gt; records = consumer.poll(Duration.ofMillis(100));\\n257:  *         for (ConsumerRecord&lt;String, String&gt; record : records) {\\n258:  *             buffer.add(record);\\n259:  *         }\\n260:  *         if (buffer.size() &gt;= minBatchSize) {\\n261:  *             insertIntoDb(buffer);\\n262:  *             consumer.commitSync();\\n263:  *             buffer.clear();\\n264:  *         }\\n265:  *     }\\n266:  * </pre>\\n267:  *\\n268:  * In this example we will consume a batch of records and batch them up in memory. When we have enough records\\n269:  * batched, we will insert them into a database. If we allowed offsets to auto commit as in the previous example, records\\n270:  * would be considered consumed after they were returned to the user in {@link #poll(Duration) poll}. It would then be\\n271:  * possible\\n272:  * for our process to fail after batching the records, but before they had been inserted into the database.\\n273:  * <p>\\n274:  * To avoid this, we will manually commit the offsets only after the corresponding records have been inserted into the\\n275:  * database. This gives us exact control of when a record is considered consumed. This raises the opposite possibility:\\n276:  * the process could fail in the interval after the insert into the database but before the commit (even though this\\n277:  * would likely just be a few milliseconds, it is a possibility). In this case the process that took over consumption\\n278:  * would consume from last committed offset and would repeat the insert of the last batch of data. Used in this way\\n279:  * Kafka provides what is often called \\\"at-least-once\\\" delivery guarantees, as each record will likely be delivered one\\n280:  * time but in failure cases could be duplicated.\\n281:  * <p>\\n282:  * <b>Note: Using automatic offset commits can also give you \\\"at-least-once\\\" delivery, but the requirement is that\\n283:  * you must consume all data returned from each call to {@link #poll(Duration)} before any subsequent calls, or before\\n284:  * {@link #close() closing} the consumer. If you fail to do either of these, it is possible for the committed offset\\n285:  * to get ahead of the consumed position, which results in missing records. The advantage of using manual offset\\n286:  * control is that you have direct control over when a record is considered \\\"consumed.\\\"</b>\\n287:  * <p>\\n288:  * The above example uses {@link #commitSync() commitSync} to mark all received records as committed. In some cases\\n289:  * you may wish to have even finer control over which records have been committed by specifying an offset explicitly.\\n290:  * In the example below we commit offset after we finish handling the records in each partition.\\n291:  * <p>\\n292:  * <pre>\\n293:  *     try {\\n294:  *         while(running) {\\n295:  *             ConsumerRecords&lt;String, String&gt; records = consumer.poll(Duration.ofMillis(Long.MAX_VALUE));\\n296:  *             for (TopicPartition partition : records.partitions()) {\\n297:  *                 List&lt;ConsumerRecord&lt;String, String&gt;&gt; partitionRecords = records.records(partition);\\n298:  *                 for (ConsumerRecord&lt;String, String&gt; record : partitionRecords) {\\n299:  *                     System.out.println(record.offset() + &quot;: &quot; + record.value());\\n300:  *                 }\\n301: \",\"url\":\"https://sourcegraph.sourcegraph.com/github.com/sourcegraph-testing/kafka@a61b998c5df7ca9baccc5d966cb1e95108cbdee9/-/blob/clients/src/main/java/org/apache/kafka/clients/consumer/KafkaConsumer.java%3FL1-300\"}",

import org.apache.kafka.common.IsolationLevel;\n36: import org.apache.kafka.common.KafkaException;\n37: import org.apache.kafka.common.Metric;\n38: import org.apache.kafka.common.MetricName;\n39: org.apache.kafka.common.errors.InvalidGroupIdException;\n43: import org.apache.kafka.common.errors.TimeoutException;\n44: import org.apache.kafka.common.internals.ClusterResourceListeners;\n45: import org.apache.kafka.common.metrics.JmxReporter;\n46: import org.apache.kafka.common.metrics.KafkaMetricsContext;\n47: import org.apache.kafka.common.metrics.MetricConfig;\n48: import org.apache.kafka.common.metrics.Sensor;\n52: import org.apache.kafka.common.network.ChannelBuilder;\n53: import org.apache.kafka.common.network.Selector;\n54: import org.apache.kafka.common.requests.MetadataRequest;\n55: import org.apache.kafka.common.serialization.Deserializer;\n56: import org.apache.kafka.common.utils.AppInfoParser;\n57: import org.apache.kafka.common.utils.LogContext;\n58: import org.apache.kafka.common.utils.Time;\n59: import org.apache.kafka.common.utils.Timer;\n60: import org.apache.kafka.common.utils.Utils;\n61: import org.slf4j.Logger;\n62: \n63: import java.net.InetSocketAddress;\n64: import java.time.Duration;\n65: import java.util.Collection;\n66: import java.util.Collections;\n67: import java.util.ConcurrentModificationException;\n68: import java.util.HashMap;\n69: import java.util.HashSet;\n70: import java.util.List;\n71: import java.util.Locale;\n72: import java.util.Map;\n73: import java.util.Objects;\n74: import java.util.Optional;\n75: import java.util.OptionalLong;\n76: import java.util.Properties;\n77: import java.util.Set;\n78: import java.util.concurrent.TimeUnit;\n79: import java.util.concurrent.atomic.AtomicInteger;\n80: import java.util.concurrent.atomic.AtomicLong;\n81: import java.util.concurrent.atomic.AtomicReference;\n82: import java.util.regex.Pattern;\n83: \n84: /**\n85:  * A client that consumes records from a Kafka cluster.\n86:  * <p>\n87:  * This client transparently handles the failure of Kafka brokers, and transparently adapts as topic partitions\n88:  * it fetches migrate within the cluster. This client also interacts with the broker to allow groups of\n89:  * consumers to load balance consumption using <a href=\"#consumergroups\">consumer groups</a>.\n90:  * <p>\n91:  * The consumer maintains TCP connections to the necessary brokers to fetch data.\n92:  * Failure to close the consumer after use will leak these connections.\n93:  * The consumer is not thread-safe. See <a href=\"#multithreaded\">Multi-threaded Processing</a> for more details.\n94:  *\n95:  * <h3>Cross-Version Compatibility</h3>\n96:  * This client can communicate with brokers that are version 0.10.0 or newer. Older or newer brokers may not support\n97:  * certain features. For example, 0.10.0 brokers do not support offsetsForTimes, because this feature was added\n98:  * in version 0.10.1. You will receive an {@link org.apache.kafka.common.errors.UnsupportedVersionException}\n99:  * when invoking an API that is not available on the running broker version.\n100:  * <p>\n101:  *\n102:  * <h3>Offsets and Consumer Position</h3>\n103:  * Kafka maintains a numerical offset for each record in a partition. This offset acts as a unique identifier of\n104:  * a record within that partition, and also denotes the position of the consumer in the partition. For example, a consumer\n105:  * which is at position 5 has consumed records with offsets 0 through 4 and will next receive the record with offset 5. There\n106:  * are actually two notions of position relevant to the user of the consumer:\n107:  * <p>\n108:  * The {@link #position(TopicPartition) position} of the consumer gives the offset of the next record that will be given\n109:  * out. It will be one larger than the highest offset the consumer has seen in that partition. It automatically advances\n110:  * every time the consumer receives messages in a call to {@link #poll(Duration)}.\n111:  * <p>\n112:  * The {@link #commitSync() committed position} is the last offset that has been stored securely. Should the\n113:  * process fail and restart, this is the offset that the consumer will recover to. The consumer can either automatically commit\n114:  * offsets periodically; or it can choose to control this committed position manually by calling one of the commit APIs\n115:  * (e.g. {@link #commitSync() commitSync} and {@link #commitAsync(OffsetCommitCallback) commitAsync}).\n116:  * <p>\n117:  * This distinction gives the consumer control over when a record is considered consumed. It is discussed in further\n118:  * detail below.\n119:  *\n120:  * <h3><a name=\"consumergroups\">Consumer Groups and Topic Subscriptions</a></h3>\n121:  *\n122:  * Kafka uses the concept of <i>consumer groups</i> to allow a pool of processes to divide the work of consuming and\n123:  * processing records. These processes can either be running on the same machine or they can be\n124:  * distributed over many machines to provide scalability and fault tolerance for processing. All consumer instances\n125:  * sharing the same {@code group.id} will be part of the same consumer group.\n126:  * <p>\n127:  * Each consumer in a group can dynamically set the list of topics it wants to subscribe to through one of the\n128:  * {@link #subscribe(Collection, ConsumerRebalanceListener) subscribe} APIs. Kafka will deliver each message in the\n129:  * subscribed topics to one process in each consumer group. This is achieved by balancing the partitions between all\n130:  * members in the consumer group so that each partition is assigned to exactly one consumer in the group. So if there\n131:  * is a topic with four partitions, and a consumer group with two processes, each process would consume from two partitions.\n132:  * <p>\n133:  * Membership in a consumer group is maintained dynamically: if a process fails, the partitions assigned to it will\n134:  * be reassigned to other consumers in the same group. Similarly, if a new consumer joins the group, partitions will be moved\n135:  * from existing consumers to the new one. This is known as <i>rebalancing</i> the group and is discussed in more\n136:  * detail <a href=\"#failuredetection\">below</a>. Group rebalancing is also used when new partitions are added\n137:  * to one of the subscribed topics or when a new topic matching a {@link #subscribe(Pattern, ConsumerRebalanceListener) subscribed regex}\n138:  * is created. The group will automatically detect the new partitions through periodic metadata refreshes and\n139:  * assign them to members of the group.\n140:  * <p>\n141:  * Conceptually you can think of a consumer group as being a single logical subscriber that happens to be made up of\n142:  * multiple processes. As a multi-subscriber system, Kafka naturally supports having any number of consumer groups for a\n143:  * given topic without duplicating data (additional consumers are actually quite cheap).\n144:  * <p>\n145:  * This is a slight generalization of the functionality that is common in messaging systems. To get semantics similar to\n146:  * a queue in a traditional messaging system all processes would be part of a single consumer group and hence record\n147:  * delivery would be balanced over the group like with a queue. Unlike a traditional messaging system, though, you can\n148:  * have multiple such groups. To get semantics similar to pub-sub in a traditional messaging system each process would\n149:  * have its own consumer group, so each process would subscribe to all the records published to the topic.\n150:  * <p>\n151:  * In addition, when group reassignment happens automatically, consumers can be notified through a {@link ConsumerRebalanceListener},\n152:  * which allows them to finish necessary application-level logic such as state cleanup, manual offset\n153:  * commits, etc. See <a href=\"#rebalancecallback\">Storing Offsets Outside Kafka</a> for more details.\n154:  * <p>\n155:  * It is also possible for the consumer to <a href=\"#manualassignment\">manually assign</a> specific partitions\n156:  * (similar to the older \"simple\" consumer) using {@link #assign(Collection)}. In this case, dynamic partition\n157:  * assignment and consumer group coordination will be disabled.\n158:  *\n159:  * <h3><a name=\"failuredetection\">Detecting Consumer Failures</a></h3>\n160:  *\n161:  * After subscribing to a set of topics, the consumer will automatically join the group when {@link #poll(Duration)} is\n162:  * invoked. The poll API is designed to ensure consumer liveness. As long as you continue to call poll, the consumer\n163:  * will stay in the group and continue to receive messages from the partitions it was assigned. Underneath the covers,\n164:  * the consumer sends periodic heartbeats to the server. If the consumer crashes or is unable to send heartbeats for\n165:  * a duration of {@code session.timeout.ms}, then the consumer will be considered dead and its partitions will\n166:  * be reassigned.\n167:  * <p>\n168:  * It is also possible that the consumer could encounter a \"livelock\" situation where it is continuing\n169:  * to send heartbeats, but no progress is being made. To prevent the consumer from holding onto its partitions\n170:  * indefinitely in this case, we provide a liveness detection mechanism using the {@code max.poll.interval.ms}\n171:  * setting. Basically if you don't call poll at least as frequently as the configured max interval,\n172:  * then the client will proactively leave the group so that another consumer can take over its partitions. When this happens,\n173:  * you may see an offset commit failure (as indicated by a {@link CommitFailedException} thrown from a call to {@link #commitSync()}).\n174:  * This is a safety mechanism which guarantees that only active members of the group are able to commit offsets.\n175:  * So to stay in the group, you must continue to call poll.\n176:  * <p>\n177:  * The consumer provides two configuration settings to control the behavior of the poll loop:\n178:  * <ol>\n179:  *     <li><code>max.poll.interval.ms</code>: By increasing the interval between expected polls, you can give\n180:  *     the consumer more time to handle a batch of records returned from {@link #poll(Duration)}. The drawback\n181:  *     is that increasing this value may delay a group rebalance since the consumer will only join the rebalance\n182:  *     inside the call to poll. You can use this setting to bound the time to finish a rebalance, but\n183:  *     you risk slower progress if the consumer cannot actually call {@link #poll(Duration) poll} often enough.</li>\n184:  *     <li><code>max.poll.records</code>: Use this setting to limit the total records returned from a single\n185:  *     call to poll. This can make it easier to predict the maximum that must be handled within each poll\n186:  *     interval. By tuning this value, you may be able to reduce the poll interval, which will reduce the\n187:  *     impact of group rebalancing.</li>\n188:  * </ol>\n189:  * <p>\n190:  * For use cases where message processing time varies unpredictably, neither of these options may be sufficient.\n191:  * The recommended way to handle these cases is to move message processing to another thread, which allows\n192:  * the consumer to continue calling {@link #poll(Duration) poll} while the processor is still working.\n193:  * Some care must be taken to ensure that committed offsets do not get ahead of the actual position.\n194:  * Typically, you must disable automatic commits and manually commit processed offsets for records only after the\n195:  * thread has finished handling them (depending on the delivery semantics you need).\n196:  * Note also that you will need to {@link #pause(Collection) pause} the partition so that no new records are received\n197:  * from poll until after thread has finished handling those previously returned.\n198:  *\n199:  * <h3>Usage Examples</h3>\n200:  * The consumer APIs offer flexibility to cover a variety of consumption use cases. Here are some examples to\n201:  * demonstrate how to use them.\n202:  *\n203:  * <h4>Automatic Offset Committing</h4>\n204:  * This example demonstrates a simple usage of Kafka's consumer api that relies on automatic offset committing.\n205:  * <p>\n206:  * <pre>\n207:  *     Properties props = new Properties();\n208:  *     props.setProperty(&quot;bootstrap.servers&quot;, &quot;localhost:9092&quot;);\n209:  *     props.setProperty(&quot;group.id&quot;, &quot;test&quot;);\n210:  *     props.setProperty(&quot;enable.auto.commit&quot;, &quot;true&quot;);\n211:  *     props.setProperty(&quot;auto.commit.interval.ms&quot;, &quot;1000&quot;);\n212:  *     props.setProperty(&quot;key.deserializer&quot;, &quot;org.apache.kafka.common.serialization.StringDeserializer&quot;);\n213:  *     props.setProperty(&quot;value.deserializer&quot;, &quot;org.apache.kafka.common.serialization.StringDeserializer&quot;);\n214:  *     KafkaConsumer&lt;String, String&gt; consumer = new KafkaConsumer&lt;&gt;(props);\n215:  *     consumer.subscribe(Arrays.asList(&quot;foo&quot;, &quot;bar&quot;));\n216:  *     while (true) {\n217:  *         ConsumerRecords&lt;String, String&gt; records = consumer.poll(Duration.ofMillis(100));\n218:  *         for (ConsumerRecord&lt;String, String&gt; record : records)\n219:  *             System.out.printf(&quot;offset = %d, key = %s, value = %s%n&quot;, record.offset(), record.key(), record.value());\n220:  *     }\n221:  * </pre>\n222:  *\n223:  * The connection to the cluster is bootstrapped by specifying a list of one or more brokers to contact using the\n224:  * configuration {@code >bootstrap.servers}. This list is just used to discover the rest of the brokers in the\n225:  * cluster and need not be an exhaustive list of servers in the cluster (though you may want to specify more than one in\n226:  * case there are servers down when the client is connecting).\n227:  * <p>\n228:  * Setting {@code enable.auto.commit} means that offsets are committed automatically with a frequency controlled by\n229:  * the config {@code auto.commit.interval.ms}.\n230:  * <p>\n231:  * In this example the consumer is subscribing to the topics <i>foo</i> and <i>bar</i> as part of a group of consumers\n232:  * called <i>test</i> as configured with {@code group.id}.\n233:  * <p>\n234:  * The deserializer settings specify how to turn bytes into objects. For example, by specifying string deserializers, we\n235:  * are saying that our record's key and value will just be simple strings.\n236:  *\n237:  * <h4>Manual Offset Control</h4>\n238:  *\n239:  * Instead of relying on the consumer to periodically commit consumed offsets, users can also control when records\n240:  * should be considered as consumed and hence commit their offsets. This is useful when the consumption of the messages\n241:  * is coupled with some processing logic and hence a message should not be considered as consumed until it is completed processing.\n242: \n243:  * <p>\n244:  * <pre>\n245:  *     Properties props = new Properties();\n246:  *     props.setProperty(&quot;bootstrap.servers&quot;, &quot;localhost:9092&quot;);\n247:  *     props.setProperty(&quot;group.id&quot;, &quot;test&quot;);\n248:  *     props.setProperty(&quot;enable.auto.commit&quot;, &quot;false&quot;);\n249:  *     props.setProperty(&quot;key.deserializer&quot;, &quot;org.apache.kafka.common.serialization.StringDeserializer&quot;);\n250:  *     props.setProperty(&quot;value.deserializer&quot;, &quot;org.apache.kafka.common.serialization.StringDeserializer&quot;);\n251:  *     KafkaConsumer&lt;String, String&gt; consumer = new KafkaConsumer&lt;&gt;(props);\n252:  *     consumer.subscribe(Arrays.asList(&quot;foo&quot;, &quot;bar&quot;));\n253:  *     final int minBatchSize = 200;\n254:  *     List&lt;ConsumerRecord&lt;String, String&gt;&gt; buffer = new ArrayList&lt;&gt;();\n255:  *     while (true) {\n256:  *         ConsumerRecords&lt;String, String&gt; records = consumer.poll(Duration.ofMillis(100));\n257:  *         for (ConsumerRecord&lt;String, String&gt; record : records) {\n258:  *             buffer.add(record);\n259:  *         }\n260:  *         if (buffer.size() &gt;= minBatchSize) {\n261:  *             insertIntoDb(buffer);\n262:  *             consumer.commitSync();\n263:  *             buffer.clear();\n264:  *         }\n265:  *     }\n266:  * </pre>\n267:  *\n268:  * In this example we will consume a batch of records and batch them up in memory. When we have enough records\n269:  * batched, we will insert them into a database. If we allowed offsets to auto commit as in the previous example, records\n270:  * would be considered consumed after they were returned to the user in {@link #poll(Duration) poll}. It would then be\n271:  * possible\n272:  * for our process to fail after batching the records, but before they had been inserted into the database.\n273:  * <p>\n274:  * To avoid this, we will manually commit the offsets only after the corresponding records have been inserted into the\n275:  * database. This gives us exact control of when a record is considered consumed. This raises the opposite possibility:\n276:  * the process could fail in the interval after the insert into the database but before the commit (even though this\n277:  * would likely just be a few milliseconds, it is a possibility). In this case the process that took over consumption\n278:  * would consume from last committed offset and would repeat the insert of the last batch of data. Used in this way\n279:  * Kafka provides what is often called \"at-least-once\" delivery guarantees, as each record will likely be delivered one\n280:  * time but in failure cases could be duplicated.\n281:  * <p>\n282:  * <b>Note: Using automatic offset commits can also give you \"at-least-once\" delivery, but the requirement is that\n283:  * you must consume all data returned from each call to {@link #poll(Duration)} before any subsequent calls, or before\n284:  * {@link #close() closing} the consumer. If you fail to do either of these, it is possible for the committed offset\n285:  * to get ahead of the consumed position, which results in missing records. The advantage of using manual offset\n286:  * control is that you have direct control over when a record is considered \"consumed.\"</b>\n287:  * <p>\n288:  * The above example uses {@link #commitSync() commitSync} to mark all received records as committed. In some cases\n289:  * you may wish to have even finer control over which records have been committed by specifying an offset explicitly.\n290:  * In the example below we commit offset after we finish handling the records in each partition.\n291:  * <p>\n292:  * <pre>\n293:  *     try {\n294:  *         while(running) {\n295:  *             ConsumerRecords&lt;String, String&gt; records = consumer.poll(Duration.ofMillis(Long.MAX_VALUE));\n296:  *             for (TopicPartition partition : records.partitions()) {\n297:  *                 List&lt;ConsumerRecord&lt;String, String&gt;&gt; partitionRecords = records.records(partition);\n298:  *                 for (ConsumerRecord&lt;String, String&gt; record : partitionRecords) {\n299:  *                     System.out.println(record.offset() + &quot;: &quot; + record.value());\n300:  *                 }\n301: ",
    "url": "https://sourcegraph.sourcegraph.com/github.com/sourcegraph-testing/kafka@a61b998c5df7ca9baccc5d966cb1e95108cbdee9/-/blob/clients/src/main/java/org/apache/kafka/clients/consumer/KafkaConsumer.java%3FL1-300"

Only `wakeup()` can be called from external threads.\n\n2. **Consumer Groups:** Multiple consumers with same `group.id` form a group, load-balancing partitions.\n\n3. **Automatic vs Manual Offset Management:**\n   - Automatic: `enable.auto.commit=true` (default)\n   - Manual: `enable.auto.commit=false`, explicit `commitSync()`/`commitAsync()`\n\n4. **Rebalancing:** Triggers when group membership changes, new partitions added, or topic deleted.\n\n5. **Offset Reset Policy:** When no offset available:\n   - `earliest`: Start from beginning\n   - `latest`: Start from end\n   - `none`: Throw exception\n\n6. **Transactional Messages:** Use `isolation.level=read_committed` to only read committed messages.\n\n7. **Multi-threaded Processing:** Use `pause()`/`resume()` or decouple consumption with producer threads.\n\n---\n\n### Source Files Referenced\n\n- `/clients/src/main/java/org/apache/kafka/clients/consumer/KafkaConsumer.java` (2506 lines)\n- `/clients/src/main/java/org/apache/kafka/clients/consumer/Consumer.java` (interface)\n- `/clients/src/main/java/org/apache/kafka/clients/consumer/ConsumerRecord.java`\n- `/clients/src/main/java/org/apache/kafka/clients/consumer/ConsumerRecords.java`\n- `/clients/src/main/java/org/apache/kafka/clients/consumer/OffsetAndMetadata.java`\n- `/clients/src/main/java/org/apache/kafka/clients/consumer/OffsetAndTimestamp.java`\n- `/clients/src/main/java/org/apache/kafka/clients/consumer/ConsumerRebalanceListener.java`\n- `/clients/src/main/java/org/apache/kafka/clients/consumer/ConsumerConfig.java`\n- `/clients/src/main/java/org/apache/kafka/clients/consumer/CommitFailedException.java`\n- `/clients/src/main/java/org/apache/kafka/clients/consumer/InvalidOffsetException.java`\n- `/clients/src/main/java/org/apache/kafka/clients/consumer/NoOffsetForPartitionException.java`\n- `/clients/src/main/java/org/apache/kafka/clients/consumer/OffsetOutOfRangeException.java`\n- `/clients/src/main/java/org/apache/kafka/clients/consumer/LogTruncationException.java`\n- `/clients/src/test/java/org/apache/kafka/clients/consumer/KafkaConsumerTest.java`\n\nThis comprehensive documentation covers all aspects of the KafkaConsumer API, including method signatures, configuration properties, exception types, and internal implementation details.",

"{\"content\":\"100:  * <p>\\n101:  *\\n102:  * <h3>Offsets and Consumer Position</h3>\\n103:  * Kafka maintains a numerical offset for each record in a partition. This offset acts as a unique identifier of\\n104:  * a record within that partition, and also denotes the position of the consumer in the partition. For example, a consumer\\n105:  * which is at position 5 has consumed records with offsets 0 through 4 and will next receive the record with offset 5. There\\n106:  * are actually two notions of position relevant to the user of the consumer:\\n107:  * <p>\\n108:  * The {@link #position(TopicPartition) position} of the consumer gives the offset of the next record that will be given\\n109:  * out. It will be one larger than the highest offset the consumer has seen in that partition. It automatically advances\\n110:  * every time the consumer receives messages in a call to {@link #poll(Duration)}.\\n111:  * <p>\\n112:  * The {@link #commitSync() committed position} is the last offset that has been stored securely. Should the\\n113:  * process fail and restart, this is the offset that the consumer will recover to. The consumer can either automatically commit\\n114:  * offsets periodically; or it can choose to control this committed position manually by calling one of the commit APIs\\n115:  * (e.g. {@link #commitSync() commitSync} and {@link #commitAsync(OffsetCommitCallback) commitAsync}).\\n116:  * <p>\\n117:  * This distinction gives the consumer control over when a record is considered consumed. It is discussed in further\\n118:  * detail below.\\n119:  *\\n120:  * <h3><a name=\\\"consumergroups\\\">Consumer Groups and Topic Subscriptions</a></h3>\\n121:  *\\n122:  * Kafka uses the concept of <i>consumer groups</i> to allow a pool of processes to divide the work of consuming and\\n123:  * processing records. These processes can either be running on the same machine or they can be\\n124:  * distributed over many machines to provide scalability and fault tolerance for processing. All consumer instances\\n125:  * sharing the same {@code group.id} will be part of the same consumer group.\\n126:  * <p>\\n127:  * Each consumer in a group can dynamically set the list of topics it wants to subscribe to through one of the\\n128:  * {@link #subscribe(Collection, ConsumerRebalanceListener) subscribe} APIs. Kafka will deliver each message in the\\n129:  * subscribed topics to one process in each consumer group. This is achieved by balancing the partitions between all\\n130:  * members in the consumer group so that each partition is assigned to exactly one consumer in the group. So if there\\n131:  * is a topic with four partitions, and a consumer group with two processes, each process would consume from two partitions.\\n132:  * <p>\\n133:  * Membership in a consumer group is maintained dynamically: if a process fails, the partitions assigned to it will\\n134:  * be reassigned to other consumers in the same group. Similarly, if a new consumer joins the group, partitions will be moved\\n135:  * from existing consumers to the new one. This is known as <i>rebalancing</i> the group and is discussed in more\\n136:  * detail <a href=\\\"#failuredetection\\\">below</a>. Group rebalancing is also used when new partitions are added\\n137:  * to one of the subscribed topics or when a new topic matching a {@link #subscribe(Pattern, ConsumerRebalanceListener) subscribed regex}\\n138:  * is created. The group will automatically detect the new partitions through periodic metadata refreshes and\\n139:  * assign them to members of the group.\\n140:  * <p>\\n141:  * Conceptually you can think of a consumer group as being a single logical subscriber that happens to be made up of\\n142:  * multiple processes. As a multi-subscriber system, Kafka naturally supports having any number of consumer groups for a\\n143:  * given topic without duplicating data (additional consumers are actually quite cheap).\\n144:  * <p>\\n145:  * This is a slight generalization of the functionality that is common in messaging systems. To get semantics similar to\\n146:  * a queue in a traditional messaging system all processes would be part of a single consumer group and hence record\\n147:  * delivery would be balanced over the group like with a queue. Unlike a traditional messaging system, though, you can\\n148:  * have multiple such groups. To get semantics similar to pub-sub in a traditional messaging system each process would\\n149:  * have its own consumer group, so each process would subscribe to all the records published to the topic.\\n150:  * <p>\\n151:  * In addition, when group reassignment happens automatically, consumers can be notified through a {@link ConsumerRebalanceListener},\\n152:  * which allows them to finish necessary application-level logic such as state cleanup, manual offset\\n153:  * commits, etc. See <a href=\\\"#rebalancecallback\\\">Storing Offsets Outside Kafka</a> for more details.\\n154:  * <p>\\n155:  * It is also possible for the consumer to <a href=\\\"#manualassignment\\\">manually assign</a> specific partitions\\n156:  * (similar to the older \\\"simple\\\" consumer) using {@link #assign(Collection)}. In this case, dynamic partition\\n157:  * assignment and consumer group coordination will be disabled.\\n158:  *\\n159:  * <h3><a name=\\\"failuredetection\\\">Detecting Consumer Failures</a></h3>\\n160:  *\\n161:  * After subscribing to a set of topics, the consumer will automatically join the group when {@link #poll(Duration)} is\\n162:  * invoked. The poll API is designed to ensure consumer liveness. As long as you continue to call poll, the consumer\\n163:  * will stay in the group and continue to receive messages from the partitions it was assigned. Underneath the covers,\\n164:  * the consumer sends periodic heartbeats to the server. If the consumer crashes or is unable to send heartbeats for\\n165:  * a duration of {@code session.timeout.ms}, then the consumer will be considered dead and its partitions will\\n166:  * be reassigned.\\n167:  * <p>\\n168:  * It is also possible that the consumer could encounter a \\\"livelock\\\" situation where it is continuing\\n169:  * to send heartbeats, but no progress is being made. To prevent the consumer from holding onto its partitions\\n170:  * indefinitely in this case, we provide a liveness detection mechanism using the {@code max.poll.interval.ms}\\n171:  * setting. Basically if you don't call poll at least as frequently as the configured max interval,\\n172:  * then the client will proactively leave the group so that another consumer can take over its partitions. When this happens,\\n173:  * you may see an offset commit failure (as indicated by a {@link CommitFailedException} thrown from a call to {@link #commitSync()}).\\n174:  * This is a safety mechanism which guarantees that only active members of the group are able to commit offsets.\\n175:  * So to stay in the group, you must continue to call poll.\\n176:  * <p>\\n177:  * The consumer provides two configuration settings to control the behavior of the poll loop:\\n178:  * <ol>\\n179:  *     <li><code>max.poll.interval.ms</code>: By increasing the interval between expected polls, you can give\\n180:  *     the consumer more time to handle a batch of records returned from {@link #poll(Duration)}. The drawback\\n181:  *     is that increasing this value may delay a group rebalance since the consumer will only join the rebalance\\n182:  *     inside the call to poll. You can use this setting to bound the time to finish a rebalance, but\\n183:  *     you risk slower progress if the consumer cannot actually call {@link #poll(Duration) poll} often enough.</li>\\n184:  *     <li><code>max.poll.records</code>: Use this setting to limit the total records returned from a single\\n185:  *     call to poll. This can make it easier to predict the maximum that must be handled within each poll\\n186:  *     interval. By tuning this value, you may be able to reduce the poll interval, which will reduce the\\n187:  *     impact of group rebalancing.</li>\\n188:  * </ol>\\n189:  * <p>\\n190:  * For use cases where message processing time varies unpredictably, neither of these options may be sufficient.\\n191:  * The recommended way to handle these cases is to move message processing to another thread, which allows\\n192:  * the consumer to continue calling {@link #poll(Duration) poll} while the processor is still working.\\n193:  * Some care must be taken to ensure that committed offsets do not get ahead of the actual position.\\n194:  * Typically, you must disable automatic commits and manually commit processed offsets for records only after the\\n195:  * thread has finished handling them (depending on the delivery semantics you need).\\n196:  * Note also that you will need to {@link #pause(Collection) pause} the partition so that no new records are received\\n197:  * from poll until after thread has finished handling those previously returned.\\n198:  *\\n199:  * <h3>Usage Examples</h3>\\n200:  * The consumer APIs offer flexibility to cover a variety of consumption use cases. Here are some examples to\\n201:  * demonstrate how to use them.\\n202:  *\\n203:  * <h4>Automatic Offset Committing</h4>\\n204:  * This example demonstrates a simple usage of Kafka's consumer api that relies on automatic offset committing.\\n205:  * <p>\\n206:  * <pre>\\n207:  *     Properties props = new Properties();\\n208:  *     props.setProperty(&quot;bootstrap.servers&quot;, &quot;localhost:9092&quot;);\\n209:  *     props.setProperty(&quot;group.id&quot;, &quot;test&quot;);\\n210:  *     props.setProperty(&quot;enable.auto.commit&quot;, &quot;true&quot;);\\n211:  *     props.setProperty(&quot;auto.commit.interval.ms&quot;, &quot;1000&quot;);\\n212:  *     props.setProperty(&quot;key.deserializer&quot;, &quot;org.apache.kafka.common.serialization.StringDeserializer&quot;);\\n213:  *     props.setProperty(&quot;value.deserializer&quot;, &quot;org.apache.kafka.common.serialization.StringDeserializer&quot;);\\n214:  *     KafkaConsumer&lt;String, String&gt; consumer = new KafkaConsumer&lt;&gt;(props);\\n215:  *     consumer.subscribe(Arrays.asList(&quot;foo&quot;, &quot;bar&quot;));\\n216:  *     while (true) {\\n217:  *         ConsumerRecords&lt;String, String&gt; records = consumer.poll(Duration.ofMillis(100));\\n218:  *         for (ConsumerRecord&lt;String, String&gt; record : records)\\n219:  *             System.out.printf(&quot;offset = %d, key = %s, value = %s%n&quot;, record.offset(), record.key(), record.value());\\n220:  *     }\\n221:  * </pre>\\n222:  *\\n223:  * The connection to the cluster is bootstrapped by specifying a list of one or more brokers to contact using the\\n224:  * configuration {@code >bootstrap.servers}. This list is just used to discover the rest of the brokers in the\\n225:  * cluster and need not be an exhaustive list of servers in the cluster (though you may want to specify more than one in\\n226:  * case there are servers down when the client is connecting).\\n227:  * <p>\\n228:  * Setting {@code enable.auto.commit} means that offsets are committed automatically with a frequency controlled by\\n229:  * the config {@code auto.commit.interval.ms}.\\n230:  * <p>\\n231:  * In this example the consumer is subscribing to the topics <i>foo</i> and <i>bar</i> as part of a group of consumers\\n232:  * called <i>test</i> as configured with {@code group.id}.\\n233:  * <p>\\n234:  * The deserializer settings specify how to turn bytes into objects. For example, by specifying string deserializers, we\\n235:  * are saying that our record's key and value will just be simple strings.\\n236:  *\\n237:  * <h4>Manual Offset Control</h4>\\n238:  *\\n239:  * Instead of relying on the consumer to periodically commit consumed offsets, users can also control when records\\n240:  * should be considered as consumed and hence commit their offsets. This is useful when the consumption of the messages\\n241:  * is coupled with some processing logic and hence a message should not be considered as consumed until it is completed processing.\\n242: \\n243:  * <p>\\n244:  * <pre>\\n245:  *     Properties props = new Properties();\\n246:  *     props.setProperty(&quot;bootstrap.servers&quot;, &quot;localhost:9092&quot;);\\n247:  *     props.setProperty(&quot;group.id&quot;, &quot;test&quot;);\\n248:  *     props.setProperty(&quot;enable.auto.commit&quot;, &quot;false&quot;);\\n249:  *     props.setProperty(&quot;key.deserializer&quot;, &quot;org.apache.kafka.common.serialization.StringDeserializer&quot;);\\n250:  *     props.setProperty(&quot;value.deserializer&quot;, &quot;org.apache.kafka.common.serialization.StringDeserializer&quot;);\\n251:  *     KafkaConsumer&lt;String, String&gt; consumer = new KafkaConsumer&lt;&gt;(props);\\n252:  *     consumer.subscribe(Arrays.asList(&quot;foo&quot;, &quot;bar&quot;));\\n253:  *     final int minBatchSize = 200;\\n254:  *     List&lt;ConsumerRecord&lt;String, String&gt;&gt; buffer = new ArrayList&lt;&gt;();\\n255:  *     while (true) {\\n256:  *         ConsumerRecords&lt;String, String&gt; records = consumer.poll(Duration.ofMillis(100));\\n257:  *         for (ConsumerRecord&lt;String, String&gt; record : records) {\\n258:  *             buffer.add(record);\\n259:  *         }\\n260:  *         if (buffer.size() &gt;= minBatchSize) {\\n261:  *             insertIntoDb(buffer);\\n262:  *             consumer.commitSync();\\n263:  *             buffer.clear();\\n264:  *         }\\n265:  *     }\\n266:  * </pre>\\n267:  *\\n268:  * In this example we will consume a batch of records and batch them up in memory. When we have enough records\\n269:  * batched, we will insert them into a database. If we allowed offsets to auto commit as in the previous example, records\\n270:  * would be considered consumed after they were returned to the user in {@link #poll(Duration) poll}. It would then be\\n271:  * possible\\n272:  * for our process to fail after batching the records, but before they had been inserted into the database.\\n273:  * <p>\\n274:  * To avoid this, we will manually commit the offsets only after the corresponding records have been inserted into the\\n275:  * database. This gives us exact control of when a record is considered consumed. This raises the opposite possibility:\\n276:  * the process could fail in the interval after the insert into the database but before the commit (even though this\\n277:  * would likely just be a few milliseconds, it is a possibility). In this case the process that took over consumption\\n278:  * would consume from last committed offset and would repeat the insert of the last batch of data. Used in this way\\n279:  * Kafka provides what is often called \\\"at-least-once\\\" delivery guarantees, as each record will likely be delivered one\\n280:  * time but in failure cases could be duplicated.\\n281:  * <p>\\n282:  * <b>Note: Using automatic offset commits can also give you \\\"at-least-once\\\" delivery, but the requirement is that\\n283:  * you must consume all data returned from each call to {@link #poll(Duration)} before any subsequent calls, or before\\n284:  * {@link #close() closing} the consumer. If you fail to do either of these, it is possible for the committed offset\\n285:  * to get ahead of the consumed position, which results in missing records. The advantage of using manual offset\\n286:  * control is that you have direct control over when a record is considered \\\"consumed.\\\"</b>\\n287:  * <p>\\n288:  * The above example uses {@link #commitSync() commitSync} to mark all received records as committed. In some cases\\n289:  * you may wish to have even finer control over which records have been committed by specifying an offset explicitly.\\n290:  * In the example below we commit offset after we finish handling the records in each partition.\\n291:  * <p>\\n292:  * <pre>\\n293:  *     try {\\n294:  *         while(running) {\\n295:  *             ConsumerRecords&lt;String, String&gt; records = consumer.poll(Duration.ofMillis(Long.MAX_VALUE));\\n296:  *             for (TopicPartition partition : records.partitions()) {\\n297:  *                 List&lt;ConsumerRecord&lt;String, String&gt;&gt; partitionRecords = records.records(partition);\\n298:  *                 for (ConsumerRecord&lt;String, String&gt; record : partitionRecords) {\\n299:  *                     System.out.println(record.offset() + &quot;: &quot; + record.value());\\n300:  *                 }\\n301: \",\"url\":\"https://sourcegraph.sourcegraph.com/github.com/sourcegraph-testing/kafka@a61b998c5df7ca9baccc5d966cb1e95108cbdee9/-/blob/clients/src/main/java/org/apache/kafka/clients/consumer/KafkaConsumer.java%3FL100-300\"}"
