However, in\\n317:  * some cases you may need finer control over the specific partitions that are assigned. For example:\\n318:  * <p>\\n319:  * <ul>\\n320:  * <li>If the process is maintaining some kind of local state associated with that partition (like a\\n321:  * local on-disk key-value store), then it should only get records for the partition it is maintaining on disk.\\n322:  * <li>If the process itself is highly available and will be restarted if it fails (perhaps using a\\n323:  * cluster management framework like YARN, Mesos, or AWS facilities, or as part of a stream processing framework). In\\n324:  * this case there is no need for Kafka to detect the failure and reassign the partition since the consuming process\\n325:  * will be restarted on another machine.\\n326:  * </ul>\\n327:  * <p>\\n328:  * To use this mode, instead of subscribing to the topic using {@link #subscribe(Collection) subscribe}, you just call\\n329:  * {@link #assign(Collection)} with the full list of partitions that you want to consume.\\n330:  *\\n331:  * <pre>\\n332:  *     String topic = &quot;foo&quot;;\\n333:  *     TopicPartition partition0 = new TopicPartition(topic, 0);\\n334:  *     TopicPartition partition1 = new TopicPartition(topic, 1);\\n335:  *     consumer.assign(Arrays.asList(partition0, partition1));\\n336:  * </pre>\\n337:  *\\n338:  * Once assigned, you can call {@link #poll(Duration) poll} in a loop, just as in the preceding examples to consume\\n339:  * records. The group that the consumer specifies is still used for committing offsets, but now the set of partitions\\n340:  * will only change with another call to {@link #assign(Collection) assign}. Manual partition assignment does\\n341:  * not use group coordination, so consumer failures will not cause assigned partitions to be rebalanced. Each consumer\\n342:  * acts independently even if it shares a groupId with another consumer. To avoid offset commit conflicts, you should\\n343:  * usually ensure that the groupId is unique for each consumer instance.\\n344:  * <p>\\n345:  * Note that it isn't possible to mix manual partition assignment (i.e. using {@link #assign(Collection) assign})\\n346:  * with dynamic partition assignment through topic subscription (i.e. using {@link #subscribe(Collection) subscribe}).\\n347:  *\\n348:  * <h4><a name=\\\"rebalancecallback\\\">Storing Offsets Outside Kafka</h4>\\n349:  *\\n350:  * The consumer application need not use Kafka's built-in offset storage, it can store offsets in a store of its own\\n351:  * choosing. The primary use case for this is allowing the application to store both the offset and the results of the\\n352:  * consumption in the same system in a way that both the results and offsets are stored atomically. This is not always\\n353:  * possible, but when it is it will make the consumption fully atomic and give \\\"exactly once\\\" semantics that are\\n354:  * stronger than the default \\\"at-least once\\\" semantics you get with Kafka's offset commit functionality.\\n355:  * <p>\\n356:  * Here are a couple of examples of this type of usage:\\n357:  * <ul>\\n358:  * <li>If the results of the consumption are being stored in a relational database, storing the offset in the database\\n359:  * as well can allow committing both the results and offset in a single transaction. Thus either the transaction will\\n360:  * succeed and the offset will be updated based on what was consumed or the result will not be stored and the offset\\n361:  * won't be updated.\\n362:  * <li>If the results are being stored in a local store it may be possible to store the offset there as well. For\\n363:  * example a search index could be built by subscribing to a particular partition and storing both the offset and the\\n364:  * indexed data together. If this is done in a way that is atomic, it is often possible to have it be the case that even\\n365:  * if a crash occurs that causes unsync'd data to be lost, whatever is left has the corresponding offset stored as well.\\n366:  * This means that in this case the indexing process that comes back having lost recent updates just resumes indexing\\n367:  * from what it has ensuring that no updates are lost.\\n368:  * </ul>\\n369:  * <p>\\n370:  * Each record comes with its own offset, so to manage your own offset you just need to do the following:\\n371:  *\\n372:  * <ul>\\n373:  * <li>Configure <code>enable.auto.commit=false</code>\\n374:  * <li>Use the offset provided with each {@link ConsumerRecord} to save your position.\\n375:  * <li>On restart restore the position of the consumer using {@link #seek(TopicPartition, long)}.\\n376:  * </ul>\\n377:  *\\n378:  * <p>\\n379:  * This type of usage is simplest when the partition assignment is also done manually (this would be likely in the\\n380:  * search index use case described above). If the partition assignment is done automatically special care is\\n381:  * needed to handle the case where partition assignments change. This can be done by providing a\\n382:  * {@link ConsumerRebalanceListener} instance in the call to {@link #subscribe(Collection, ConsumerRebalanceListener)}\\n383:  * and {@link #subscribe(Pattern, ConsumerRebalanceListener)}.\\n384:  * For example, when partitions are taken from a consumer the consumer will want to commit its offset for those partitions by\\n385:  * implementing {@link ConsumerRebalanceListener#onPartitionsRevoked(Collection)}. When partitions are assigned to a\\n386:  * consumer, the consumer will want to look up the offset for those new partitions and correctly initialize the consumer\\n387:  * to that position by implementing {@link ConsumerRebalanceListener#onPartitionsAssigned(Collection)}.\\n388:  * <p>\\n389:  * Another common use for {@link ConsumerRebalanceListener} is to flush any caches the application maintains for\\n390:  * partitions that are moved elsewhere.\\n391:  *\\n392:  * <h4>Controlling The Consumer's Position</h4>\\n393:  *\\n394:  * In most use cases the consumer will simply consume records from beginning to end, periodically committing its\\n395:  * position (either automatically or manually). However Kafka allows the consumer to manually control its position,\\n396:  * moving forward or backwards in a partition at will. This means a consumer can re-consume older records, or skip to\\n397:  * the most recent records without actually consuming the intermediate records.\\n398:  * <p>\\n399:  * There are several instances where manually controlling the consumer's position can be useful.\\n400:  * <p>\\n401:  * One case is for time-sensitive record processing it may make sense for a consumer that falls far enough behind to not\\n402:  * attempt to catch up processing all records, but rather just skip to the most recent records.\\n403:  * <p>\\n404:  * Another use case is for a system that maintains local state as described in the previous section. In such a system\\n405:  * the consumer will want to initialize its position on start-up to whatever is contained in the local store. Likewise\\n406:  * if the local state is destroyed (say because the disk is lost) the state may be recreated on a new machine by\\n407:  * re-consuming all the data and recreating the state (assuming that Kafka is retaining sufficient history).\\n408:  * <p>\\n409:  * Kafka allows specifying the position using {@link #seek(TopicPartition, long)} to specify the new position. Special\\n410:  * methods for seeking to the earliest and latest offset the server maintains are also available (\\n411:  * {@link #seekToBeginning(Collection)} and {@link #seekToEnd(Collection)} respectively).\\n412:  *\\n413:  * <h4>Consumption Flow Control</h4>\\n414:  *\\n415:  * If a consumer is assigned multiple partitions to fetch data from, it will try to consume from all of them at the same time,\\n416:  * effectively giving these partitions the same priority for consumption. However in some cases consumers may want to\\n417:  * first focus on fetching from some subset of the assigned partitions at full speed, and only start fetching other partitions\\n418:  * when these partitions have few or no data to consume.\\n419:  *\\n420:  * <p>\\n421:  * One of such cases is stream processing, where processor fetches from two topics and performs the join on these two streams.\\n422:  * When one of the topics is long lagging behind the other, the processor would like to pause fetching from the ahead topic\\n423:  * in order to get the lagging stream to catch up. Another example is bootstraping upon consumer starting up where there are\\n424:  * a lot of history data to catch up, the applications usually want to get the latest data on some of the topics before consider\\n425:  * fetching other topics.\\n426:  *\\n427:  * <p>\\n428:  * Kafka supports dynamic controlling of consumption flows by using {@link #pause(Collection)} and {@link #resume(Collection)}\\n429:  * to pause the consumption on the specified assigned partitions and resume the consumption\\n430:  * on the specified paused partitions respectively in the future {@link #poll(Duration)} calls.\\n431:  *\\n432:  * <h3>Reading Transactional Messages</h3>\\n433:  *\\n434:  * <p>\\n435:  * Transactions were introduced in Kafka 0.11.0 wherein applications can write to multiple topics and partitions atomically.\\n436:  * In order for this to work, consumers reading from these partitions should be configured to only read committed data.\\n437:  * This can be achieved by setting the {@code isolation.level=read_committed} in the consumer's configuration.\\n438:  *\\n439:  * <p>\\n440:  * In <code>read_committed</code> mode, the consumer will read only those transactional messages which have been\\n441:  * successfully committed. It will continue to read non-transactional messages as before. There is no client-side\\n442:  * buffering in <code>read_committed</code> mode. Instead, the end offset of a partition for a <code>read_committed</code>\\n443:  * consumer would be the offset of the first message in the partition belonging to an open transaction. This offset\\n444:  * is known as the 'Last Stable Offset'(LSO).</p>\\n445:  *\\n446:  * <p>\\n447:  * A {@code read_committed} consumer will only read up to the LSO and filter out any transactional\\n448:  * messages which have been aborted. The LSO also affects the behavior of {@link #seekToEnd(Collection)} and\\n449:  * {@link #endOffsets(Collection)} for {@code read_committed} consumers, details of which are in each method's documentation.\\n450:  * Finally, the fetch lag metrics are also adjusted to be relative to the LSO for {@code read_committed} consumers.\\n451:  *\\n452:  * <p>\\n453:  * Partitions with transactional messages will include commit or abort markers which indicate the result of a transaction.\\n454:  * There markers are not returned to applications, yet have an offset in the log. As a result, applications reading from\\n455:  * topics with transactional messages will see gaps in the consumed offsets. These missing messages would be the transaction\\n456:  * markers, and they are filtered out for consumers in both isolation levels. Additionally, applications using\\n457:  * {@code read_committed} consumers may also see gaps due to aborted transactions, since those messages would not\\n458:  * be returned by the consumer and yet would have valid offsets.\\n459:  *\\n460:  * <h3><a name=\\\"multithreaded\\\">Multi-threaded Processing</a></h3>\\n461:  *\\n462:  * The Kafka consumer is NOT thread-safe. All network I/O happens in the thread of the application\\n463:  * making the call. It is the responsibility of the user to ensure that multi-threaded access\\n464:  * is properly synchronized. Un-synchronized access will result in {@link ConcurrentModificationException}.\\n465:  *\\n466:  * <p>\\n467:  * The only exception to this rule is {@link #wakeup()}, which can safely be used from an external thread to\\n468:  * interrupt an active operation. In this case, a {@link org.apache.kafka.common.errors.WakeupException} will be\\n469:  * thrown from the thread blocking on the operation. This can be used to shutdown the consumer from another thread.\\n470:  * The following snippet shows the typical pattern:\\n471:  *\\n472:  * <pre>\\n473:  * public class KafkaConsumerRunner implements Runnable {\\n474:  *     private final AtomicBoolean closed = new AtomicBoolean(false);\\n475:  *     private final KafkaConsumer consumer;\\n476:  *\\n477:  *     public KafkaConsumerRunner(KafkaConsumer consumer) {\\n478:  *       this.consumer = consumer;\\n479:  *     }\\n480:  *\\n481:  *     {@literal}@Override\\n482:  *     public void run() {\\n483:  *         try {\\n484:  *             consumer.subscribe(Arrays.asList(\\\"topic\\\"));\\n485:  *             while (!closed.get()) {\\n486:  *                 ConsumerRecords records = consumer.poll(Duration.ofMillis(10000));\\n487:  *                 // Handle new records\\n488:  *             }\\n489:  *         } catch (WakeupException e) {\\n490:  *             // Ignore exception if closing\\n491:  *             if (!closed.get()) throw e;\\n492:  *         } finally {\\n493:  *             consumer.close();\\n494:  *         }\\n495:  *     }\\n496:  *\\n497:  *     // Shutdown hook which can be called from a separate thread\\n498:  *     public void shutdown() {\\n499:  *         closed.set(true);\\n500:  *         consumer.wakeup();\\n501: \",\"url\":\"https://sourcegraph.sourcegraph.com/github.com/sourcegraph-testing/kafka@a61b998c5df7ca9baccc5d966cb1e95108cbdee9/-/blob/clients/src/main/java/org/apache/kafka/clients/consumer/KafkaConsumer.java%3FL300-500\"}"

Interrupts are mainly supported for those cases where using {@link #wakeup()}\\n517:  * is impossible, e.g. when a consumer thread is managed by code that is unaware of the Kafka client.\\n518:  *\\n519:  * <p>\\n520:  * We have intentionally avoided implementing a particular threading model for processing. This leaves several\\n521:  * options for implementing multi-threaded processing of records.\\n522:  *\\n523:  * <h4>1. One Consumer Per Thread</h4>\\n524:  *\\n525:  * A simple option is to give each thread its own consumer instance. Here are the pros and cons of this approach:\\n526:  * <ul>\\n527:  * <li><b>PRO</b>: It is the easiest to implement\\n528:  * <li><b>PRO</b>: It is often the fastest as no inter-thread co-ordination is needed\\n529:  * <li><b>PRO</b>: It makes in-order processing on a per-partition basis very easy to implement (each thread just\\n530:  * processes messages in the order it receives them).\\n531:  * <li><b>CON</b>: More consumers means more TCP connections to the cluster (one per thread). In general Kafka handles\\n532:  * connections very efficiently so this is generally a small cost.\\n533:  * <li><b>CON</b>: Multiple consumers means more requests being sent to the server and slightly less batching of data\\n534:  * which can cause some drop in I/O throughput.\\n535:  * <li><b>CON</b>: The number of total threads across all processes will be limited by the total number of partitions.\\n536:  * </ul>\\n537:  *\\n538:  * <h4>2. Decouple Consumption and Processing</h4>\\n539:  *\\n540:  * Another alternative is to have one or more consumer threads that do all data consumption and hands off\\n541:  * {@link ConsumerRecords} instances to a blocking queue consumed by a pool of processor threads that actually handle\\n542:  * the record processing.\\n543:  *\\n544:  * This option likewise has pros and cons:\\n545:  * <ul>\\n546:  * <li><b>PRO</b>: This option allows independently scaling the number of consumers and processors. This makes it\\n547:  * possible to have a single consumer that feeds many processor threads, avoiding any limitation on partitions.\\n548:  * <li><b>CON</b>: Guaranteeing order across the processors requires particular care as the threads will execute\\n549:  * independently an earlier chunk of data may actually be processed after a later chunk of data just due to the luck of\\n550:  * thread execution timing. For processing that has no ordering requirements this is not a problem.\\n551:  * <li><b>CON</b>: Manually committing the position becomes harder as it requires that all threads co-ordinate to ensure\\n552:  * that processing is complete for that partition.\\n553:  * </ul>\\n554:  *\\n555:  * There are many possible variations on this approach. For example each processor thread can have its own queue, and\\n556:  * the consumer threads can hash into these queues using the TopicPartition to ensure in-order consumption and simplify\\n557:  * commit.\\n558:  */\\n559: public class KafkaConsumer<K, V> implements Consumer<K, V> {\\n560: \\n561:     private static final String CLIENT_ID_METRIC_TAG = \\\"client-id\\\";\\n562:     private "{\"content\":\"1: /*\\n2:  * Licensed to the Apache Software Foundation (ASF) under one or more\\n3:  * contributor license agreements. See the NOTICE file distributed with\\n4:  * this work for additional information regarding copyright ownership.\\n5:  * The ASF licenses this file to You under the Apache License, Version 2.0\\n6:  * (the \\\"License\\\"); you may not use this file except in compliance with\\n7:  * the License. You may obtain a copy of the License at\\n8:  *\\n9:  *    http://www.apache.org/licenses/LICENSE-2.0\\n10:  *\\n11:  * Unless required by applicable law or agreed to in writing, software\\n12:  * distributed under the License is distributed on an \\\"AS IS\\\" BASIS,\\n13:  * WITHOUT WARRANTIES OR CONDITIONS OF ANY KIND, either express or implied.\\n14:  * See the License for the specific language governing permissions and\\n15:  * limitations under the License.\\n16:  */\\n17: package org.apache.kafka.clients.consumer;\\n18: \\n19: import java.time.Duration;\\n20: import java.util.Collection;\\n21: \\n22: import org.apache.kafka.common.TopicPartition;\\n23: \\n24: /**\\n25:  * A callback interface that the user can implement to trigger custom actions when the set of partitions assigned to the\\n26:  * consumer changes.\\n27:  * <p>\\n28:  * This is applicable when the consumer is having Kafka auto-manage group membership. If the consumer directly assigns partitions,\\n29:  * those partitions will never be reassigned and this callback is not applicable.\\n30:  * <p>\\n31:  * When Kafka is managing the group membership, a partition re-assignment will be triggered any time the members of the group change or the subscription\\n32:  * of the members changes. This can occur when processes die, new process instances are added or old instances come back to life after failure.\\n33:  * Partition re-assignments can also be triggered by changes affecting the subscribed topics (e.g. when the number of partitions is\\n34:  * administratively adjusted).\\n35:  * <p>\\n36:  * There are many uses for this functionality. One common use is saving offsets in a custom store. By saving offsets in\\n37:  * the {@link #onPartitionsRevoked(Collection)} call we can ensure that any time partition assignment changes\\n38:  * the offset gets saved.\\n39:  * <p>\\n40:  * Another use is flushing out any kind of cache of intermediate results the consumer may be keeping. For example,\\n41:  * consider a case where the consumer is subscribed to a topic containing user page views, and the goal is to count the\\n42:  * number of page views per user for each five minute window. Let's say the topic is partitioned by the user id so that\\n43:  * all events for a particular user go to a single consumer instance. The consumer can keep in memory a running\\n44:  * tally of actions per user and only flush these out to a remote data store when its cache gets too big. However if a\\n45:  * partition is reassigned it may want to automatically trigger a flush of this cache, before the new owner takes over\\n46:  * consumption.\\n47:  * <p>\\n48:  * This callback will only execute in the user thread as part of the {@link Consumer#poll(java.time.Duration) poll(long)} call\\n49:  * whenever partition assignment changes.\\n50:  * <p>\\n51:  * Under normal conditions, if a partition is reassigned from one consumer to another, then the old consumer will\\n52:  * always invoke {@link #onPartitionsRevoked(Collection) onPartitionsRevoked} for that partition prior to the new consumer\\n53:  * invoking {@link #onPartitionsAssigned(Collection) onPartitionsAssigned} for the same partition. So if offsets or other state is saved in the\\n54:  * {@link #onPartitionsRevoked(Collection) onPartitionsRevoked} call by one consumer member, it will be always accessible by the time the\\n55:  * other consumer member taking over that partition and triggering its {@link #onPartitionsAssigned(Collection) onPartitionsAssigned} callback to load the state.\\n56:  * <p>\\n57:  * You can think of revocation as a graceful way to give up ownership of a partition. In some cases, the consumer may not have an opportunity to do so.\\n58:  * For example, if the session times out, then the partitions may be reassigned before we have a chance to revoke them gracefully.\\n59:  * For this case, we have a third callback {@link #onPartitionsLost(Collection)}. The difference between this function and\\n60:  * {@link #onPartitionsRevoked(Collection)} is that upon invocation of {@link #onPartitionsLost(Collection)}, the partitions\\n61:  * may already be owned by some other members in the group and therefore users would not be able to commit its consumed offsets for example.\\n62:  * Users could implement these two functions differently (by default,\\n63:  * {@link #onPartitionsLost(Collection)} will be calling {@link #onPartitionsRevoked(Collection)} directly); for example, in the\\n64:  * {@link #onPartitionsLost(Collection)} we should not need to store the offsets since we know these partitions are no longer owned by the consumer\\n65:  * at that time.\\n66:  * <p>\\n67:  * During a rebalance event, the {@link #onPartitionsAssigned(Collection) onPartitionsAssigned} function will always be triggered exactly once when\\n68:  * the rebalance completes. That is, even if there is no newly assigned partitions for a consumer member, its {@link #onPartitionsAssigned(Collection) onPartitionsAssigned}\\n69:  * will still be triggered with an empty collection of partitions. As a result this function can be used also to notify when a rebalance event has happened.\\n70:  * With eager rebalancing, {@link #onPartitionsRevoked(Collection)} will always be called at the start of a rebalance. On the other hand, {@link #onPartitionsLost(Collection)}\\n71:  * will only be called when there were non-empty partitions that were lost.\\n72:  * With cooperative rebalancing, {@link #onPartitionsRevoked(Collection)} and {@link #onPartitionsLost(Collection)}\\n73:  * will only be triggered when there are non-empty partitions revoked or lost from this consumer member during a rebalance event.\\n74:  * <p>\\n75:  * It is possible\\n76:  * for a {@link org.apache.kafka.common.errors.WakeupException} or {@link org.apache.kafka.common.errors.InterruptException}\\n77:  * to be raised from one of these nested invocations. In this case, the exception will be propagated to the current\\n78:  * invocation of {@link KafkaConsumer#poll(java.time.Duration)} in which this callback is being executed. This means it is not\\n79:  * necessary to catch these exceptions and re-attempt to wakeup or interrupt the consumer thread.\\n80:  * Also if the callback function implementation itself throws an exception, this exception will be propagated to the current\\n81:  * invocation of {@link KafkaConsumer#poll(java.time.Duration)} as well.\\n82:  * <p>\\n83:  * Note that callbacks only serve as notification of an assignment change.\\n84:  * They cannot be used to express acceptance of the change.\\n85:  * Hence throwing an exception from a callback does not affect the assignment in any way,\\n86:  * as it will be propagated all the way up to the {@link KafkaConsumer#poll(java.time.Duration)} call.\\n87:  * If user captures the exception in the caller, the callback is still assumed successful and no further retries will be attempted.\\n88:  * <p>\\n89:  *\\n90:  * Here is pseudo-code for a callback implementation for saving offsets:\\n91:  * <pre>\\n92:  * {@code\\n93:  *   public class SaveOffsetsOnRebalance implements ConsumerRebalanceListener {\\n94:  *       private Consumer<?,?> consumer;\\n95:  *\\n96:  *       public SaveOffsetsOnRebalance(Consumer<?,?> consumer) {\\n97:  *           this.consumer = consumer;\\n98:  *       }\\n99:  *\\n100:  *       public void onPartitionsRevoked(Collection<TopicPartition> partitions) {\\n101:  *           // save the offsets in an external store using some custom code not described here\\n102:  *           for(TopicPartition partition: partitions)\\n103:  *              saveOffsetInExternalStore(consumer.position(partition));\\n104:  *       }\\n105:  *\\n106:  *       public void onPartitionsLost(Collection<TopicPartition> partitions) {\\n107:  *           // do not need to save the offsets since these partitions are probably owned by other consumers already\\n108:  *       }\\n109:  *\\n110:  *       public void onPartitionsAssigned(Collection<TopicPartition> partitions) {\\n111:  *           // read the offsets from an external store using some custom code not described here\\n112:  *           for(TopicPartition partition: partitions)\\n113:  *              consumer.seek(partition, readOffsetFromExternalStore(partition));\\n114:  *       }\\n115:  *   }\\n116:  * }\\n117:  * </pre>\\n118:  */\\n119: public interface ConsumerRebalanceListener {\\n120: \\n121:     /**\\n122:      * A callback method the user can implement to provide handling of offset commits to a customized store.\\n123:      * This method will be called during a rebalance operation when the consumer has to give up some partitions.\\n124:      * It can also be called when consumer is being closed ({@link KafkaConsumer#close(Duration)})\\n125:      * or is unsubscribing ({@link KafkaConsumer#unsubscribe()}).\\n126:      * It is recommended that offsets should be committed in this callback to either Kafka or a\\n127:      * custom offset store to prevent duplicate data.\\n128:      * <p>\\n129:      * In eager rebalancing, it will always be called at the start of a rebalance and after the consumer stops fetching data.\\n130:      * In cooperative rebalancing, it will be called at the end of a rebalance on the set of partitions being revoked iff the set is non-empty.\\n131:      * For examples on usage of this API, see Usage Examples section of {@link KafkaConsumer KafkaConsumer}.\\n132:      * <p>\\n133:      * It is common for the revocation callback to use the consumer instance in order to commit offsets. It is possible\\n134:      * for a {@link org.apache.kafka.common.errors.WakeupException} or {@link org.apache.kafka.common.errors.InterruptException}\\n135:      * to be raised from one of these nested invocations. In this case, the exception will be propagated to the current\\n136:      * invocation of {@link KafkaConsumer#poll(java.time.Duration)} in which this callback is being executed. This means it is not\\n137:      * necessary to catch these exceptions and re-attempt to wakeup or interrupt the consumer thread.\\n138:      *\\n139:      * @param partitions The list of partitions that were assigned to the consumer and now need to be revoked (may not\\n140:      *                   include all currently assigned partitions, i.e. there may still be some partitions left)\\n141:      * @throws org.apache.kafka.common.errors.WakeupException If raised from a nested call to {@link KafkaConsumer}\\n142:      * @throws org.apache.kafka.common.errors.InterruptException If raised from a nested call to {@link KafkaConsumer}\\n143:      */\\n144:     void onPartitionsRevoked(Collection<TopicPartition> partitions);\\n145: \\n146:     /**\\n147:      * A callback method the user can implement to provide handling of customized offsets on completion of a successful\\n148:      * partition re-assignment. This method will be called after the partition re-assignment completes and before the\\n149:      * consumer starts fetching data, and only as the result of a {@link Consumer#poll(java.time.Duration) poll(long)} call.\\n150:      * <p>\\n151:      * It is guaranteed that under normal conditions all the processes in a consumer group will execute their\\n152:      * {@link #onPartitionsRevoked(Collection)} callback before any instance executes its\\n153:      * {@link #onPartitionsAssigned(Collection)} callback. During exceptional scenarios, partitions may be migrated\\n154:      * without the old owner being notified (i.e. their {@link #onPartitionsRevoked(Collection)} callback not triggered),\\n155:      * and later when the old owner consumer realized this event, the {@link #onPartitionsLost(Collection)} (Collection)} callback\\n156:      * will be triggered by the consumer then.\\n157:      * <p>\\n158:      * It is common for the assignment callback to use the consumer instance in order to query offsets. It is possible\\n159:      * for a {@link org.apache.kafka.common.errors.WakeupException} or {@link org.apache.kafka.common.errors.InterruptException}\\n160:      * to be raised from one of these nested invocations. In this case, the exception will be propagated to the current\\n161:      * invocation of {@link KafkaConsumer#poll(java.time.Duration)} in which this callback is being executed. This means it is not\\n162:      * necessary to catch these exceptions and re-attempt to wakeup or interrupt the consumer thread.\\n163:      *\\n164:      * @param partitions The list of partitions that are now assigned to the consumer (previously owned partitions will\\n165:      *                   NOT be included, i.e. this list will only include newly added partitions)\\n166:      * @throws org.apache.kafka.common.errors.WakeupException If raised from a nested call to {@link KafkaConsumer}\\n167:      * @throws org.apache.kafka.common.errors.InterruptException If raised from a nested call to {@link KafkaConsumer}\\n168:      */\\n169:     void onPartitionsAssigned(Collection<TopicPartition> partitions);\\n170: \\n171:     /**\\n172:      * A callback method you can implement to provide handling of cleaning up resources for partitions that have already\\n173:      * been reassigned to other consumers. This method will not be called during normal execution as the owned partitions would\\n174:      * first be revoked by calling the {@link ConsumerRebalanceListener#onPartitionsRevoked}, before being reassigned\\n175:      * to other consumers during a rebalance event. However, during exceptional scenarios when the consumer realized that it\\n176:      * does not own this partition any longer, i.e. not revoked via a normal rebalance event, then this method would be invoked.\\n177:      * <p>\\n178:      * For example, this function is called if a consumer's session timeout has expired, or if a fatal error has been\\n179:      * received indicating the consumer is no longer part of the group.\\n180:      * <p>\\n181:      * By default it will just trigger {@link ConsumerRebalanceListener#onPartitionsRevoked}; for users who want to distinguish\\n182:      * the handling logic of revoked partitions v.s. lost partitions, they can override the default implementation.\\n183:      * <p>\\n184:      * It is possible\\n185:      * for a {@link org.apache.kafka.common.errors.WakeupException} or {@link org.apache.kafka.common.errors.InterruptException}\\n186:      * to be raised from one of these nested invocations. You may obtain a copy of the License at\\n8:  *\\n9:  *    http://www.apache.org/licenses/LICENSE-2.0\\n10:  *\\n11:  * Unless required by applicable law or agreed to in writing, software\\n12:  * distributed under the License is distributed on an \\\"AS IS\\\" BASIS,\\n13:  * WITHOUT WARRANTIES OR CONDITIONS OF ANY KIND, either express or implied.\\n14:  * See the License for the specific language governing permissions and\\n15:  * limitations under the License.\\n16:  */\\n17: package org.apache.kafka.clients.consumer;\\n18: \\n19: import java.time.Duration;\\n20: import java.util.Collection;\\n21: \\n22: import org.apache.kafka.common.TopicPartition;\\n23: \\n24: /**\\n25:  * A callback interface that the user can implement to trigger custom actions when the set of partitions assigned to the\\n26:  * consumer changes.\\n27:  * <p>\\n28:  * This is applicable when the consumer is having Kafka auto-manage group membership. If the consumer directly assigns partitions,\\n29:  * those partitions will never be reassigned and this callback is not applicable.\\n30:  * <p>\\n31:  * When Kafka is managing the group membership, a partition re-assignment will be triggered any time the members of the group change or the subscription\\n32:  * of the members changes. This can occur when processes die, new process instances are added or old instances come back to life after failure.\\n33:  * Partition re-assignments can also be triggered by changes affecting the subscribed topics (e.g. when the number of partitions is\\n34:  * administratively adjusted).\\n35:  * <p>\\n36:  * There are many uses for this functionality. One common use is saving offsets in a custom store. By saving offsets in\\n37:  * the {@link #onPartitionsRevoked(Collection)} call we can ensure that any time partition assignment changes\\n38:  * the offset gets saved.\\n39:  * <p>\\n40:  * Another use is flushing out any kind of cache of intermediate results the consumer may be keeping. For example,\\n41:  * consider a case where the consumer is subscribed to a topic containing user page views, and the goal is to count the\\n42:  * number of page views per user for each five minute window. Let's say the topic is partitioned by the user id so that\\n43:  * all events for a particular user go to a single consumer instance. The consumer can keep in memory a running\\n44:  * tally of actions per user and only flush these out to a remote data store when its cache gets too big. However if a\\n45:  * partition is reassigned it may want to automatically trigger a flush of this cache, before the new owner takes over\\n46:  * consumption.\\n47:  * <p>\\n48:  * This callback will only execute in the user thread as part of the {@link Consumer#poll(java.time.Duration) poll(long)} call\\n49:  * whenever partition assignment changes.\\n50:  * <p>\\n51:  * Under normal conditions, if a partition is reassigned from one consumer to another, then the old consumer will\\n52:  * always invoke {@link #onPartitionsRevoked(Collection) onPartitionsRevoked} for that partition prior to the new consumer\\n53:  * invoking {@link #onPartitionsAssigned(Collection) onPartitionsAssigned} for the same partition. So if offsets or other state is saved in the\\n54:  * {@link #onPartitionsRevoked(Collection) onPartitionsRevoked} call by one consumer member, it will be always accessible by the time the\\n55:  * other consumer member taking over that partition and triggering its {@link #onPartitionsAssigned(Collection) onPartitionsAssigned} callback to load the state.\\n56:  * <p>\\n57:  * You can think of revocation as a graceful way to give up ownership of a partition. In some cases, the consumer may not have an opportunity to do so.\\n58:  * For example, if the session times out, then the partitions may be reassigned before we have a chance to revoke them gracefully.\\n59:  * For this case, we have a third callback {@link #onPartitionsLost(Collection)}. The difference between this function and\\n60:  * {@link #onPartitionsRevoked(Collection)} is that upon invocation of {@link #onPartitionsLost(Collection)}, the partitions\\n61:  * may already be owned by some other members in the group and therefore users would not be able to commit its consumed offsets for example.\\n62:  * Users could implement these two functions differently (by default,\\n63:  * {@link #onPartitionsLost(Collection)} will be calling {@link #onPartitionsRevoked(Collection)} directly); for example, in the\\n64:  * {@link #onPartitionsLost(Collection)} we should not need to store the offsets since we know these partitions are no longer owned by the consumer\\n65:  * at that time.\\n66:  * <p>\\n67:  * During a rebalance event, the {@link #onPartitionsAssigned(Collection) onPartitionsAssigned} function will always be triggered exactly once when\\n68:  * the rebalance completes. That is, even if there is no newly assigned partitions for a consumer member, its {@link #onPartitionsAssigned(Collection) onPartitionsAssigned}\\n69:  * will still be triggered with an empty collection of partitions. As a result this function can be used also to notify when a rebalance event has happened.\\n70:  * With eager rebalancing, {@link #onPartitionsRevoked(Collection)} will always be called at the start of a rebalance. On the other hand, {@link #onPartitionsLost(Collection)}\\n71:  * will only be called when there were non-empty partitions that were lost.\\n72:  * With cooperative rebalancing, {@link #onPartitionsRevoked(Collection)} and {@link #onPartitionsLost(Collection)}\\n73:  * will only be triggered when there are non-empty partitions revoked or lost from this consumer member during a rebalance event.\\n74:  * <p>\\n75:  * It is possible\\n76:  * for a {@link org.apache.kafka.common.errors.WakeupException} or {@link org.apache.kafka.common.errors.InterruptException}\\n77:  * to be raised from one of these nested invocations. In this case, the exception will be propagated to the current\\n78:  * invocation of {@link KafkaConsumer#poll(java.time.Duration)} in which this callback is being executed. This means it is not\\n79:  * necessary to catch these exceptions and re-attempt to wakeup or interrupt the consumer thread.\\n80:  * Also if the callback function implementation itself throws an exception, this exception will be propagated to the current\\n81:  * invocation of {@link KafkaConsumer#poll(java.time.Duration)} as well.\\n82:  * <p>\\n83:  * Note that callbacks only serve as notification of an assignment change.\\n84:  * They cannot be used to express acceptance of the change.\\n85:  * Hence throwing an exception from a callback does not affect the assignment in any way,\\n86:  * as it will be propagated all the way up to the {@link KafkaConsumer#poll(java.time.Duration)} call.\\n87:  * If user captures the exception in the caller, the callback is still assumed successful and no further retries will be attempted.\\n88:  * <p>\\n89:  *\\n90:  * Here is pseudo-code for a callback implementation for saving offsets:\\n91:  * <pre>\\n92:  * {@code\\n93:  *   public class SaveOffsetsOnRebalance implements ConsumerRebalanceListener {\\n94:  *       private Consumer<?,?> consumer;\\n95:  *\\n96:  *       public SaveOffsetsOnRebalance(Consumer<?,?> consumer) {\\n97:  *           this.consumer = consumer;\\n98:  *       }\\n99:  *\\n100:  *       public void onPartitionsRevoked(Collection<TopicPartition> partitions) {\\n101:  *           // save the offsets in an external store using some custom code not described here\\n102:  *           for(TopicPartition partition: partitions)\\n103:  *              saveOffsetInExternalStore(consumer.position(partition));\\n104:  *       }\\n105:  *\\n106:  *       public void onPartitionsLost(Collection<TopicPartition> partitions) {\\n107:  *           // do not need to save the offsets since these partitions are probably owned by other consumers already\\n108:  *       }\\n109:  *\\n110:  *       public void onPartitionsAssigned(Collection<TopicPartition> partitions) {\\n111:  *           // read the offsets from an external store using some custom code not described here\\n112:  *           for(TopicPartition partition: partitions)\\n113:  *              consumer.seek(partition, readOffsetFromExternalStore(partition));\\n114:  *       }\\n115:  *   }\\n116:  * }\\n117:  * </pre>\\n118:  */\\n119: public interface ConsumerRebalanceListener {\\n120: \\n121:     /**\\n122:      * A callback method the user can implement to provide handling of offset commits to a customized store.\\n123:      * This method will be called during a rebalance operation when the consumer has to give up some partitions.\\n124:      * It can also be called when consumer is being closed ({@link KafkaConsumer#close(Duration)})\\n125:      * or is unsubscribing ({@link KafkaConsumer#unsubscribe()}).\\n126:      * It is recommended that offsets should be committed in this callback to either Kafka or a\\n127:      * custom offset store to prevent duplicate data.\\n128:      * <p>\\n129:      * In eager rebalancing, it will always be called at the start of a rebalance and after the consumer stops fetching data.\\n130:      * In cooperative rebalancing, it will be called at the end of a rebalance on the set of partitions being revoked iff the set is non-empty.\\n131:      * For examples on usage of this API, see Usage Examples section of {@link KafkaConsumer KafkaConsumer}.\\n132:      * <p>\\n133:      * It is common for the revocation callback to use the consumer instance in order to commit offsets. It is possible\\n134:      * for a {@link org.apache.kafka.common.errors.WakeupException} or {@link org.apache.kafka.common.errors.InterruptException}\\n135:      * to be raised from one of these nested invocations. In this case, the exception will be propagated to the current\\n136:      * invocation of {@link KafkaConsumer#poll(java.time.Duration)} in which this callback is being executed. This means it is not\\n137:      * necessary to catch these exceptions and re-attempt to wakeup or interrupt the consumer thread.\\n138:      *\\n139:      * @param partitions The list of partitions that were assigned to the consumer and now need to be revoked (may not\\n140:      *                   include all currently assigned partitions, i.e. there may still be some partitions left)\\n141:      * @throws org.apache.kafka.common.errors.WakeupException If raised from a nested call to {@link KafkaConsumer}\\n142:      * @throws org.apache.kafka.common.errors.InterruptException If raised from a nested call to {@link KafkaConsumer}\\n143:      */\\n144:     void onPartitionsRevoked(Collection<TopicPartition> partitions);\\n145: \\n146:     /**\\n147:      * A callback method the user can implement to provide handling of customized offsets on completion of a successful\\n148:      * partition re-assignment. This method will be called after the partition re-assignment completes and before the\\n149:      * consumer starts fetching data, and only as the result of a {@link Consumer#poll(java.time.Duration) poll(long)} call.\\n150:      * <p>\\n151:      * It is guaranteed that under normal conditions all the processes in a consumer group will execute their\\n152:      * {@link #onPartitionsRevoked(Collection)} callback before any instance executes its\\n153:      * {@link #onPartitionsAssigned(Collection)} callback. During exceptional scenarios, partitions may be migrated\\n154:      * without the old owner being notified (i.e. their {@link #onPartitionsRevoked(Collection)} callback not triggered),\\n155:      * and later when the old owner consumer realized this event, the {@link #onPartitionsLost(Collection)} (Collection)} callback\\n156:      * will be triggered by the consumer then.\\n157:      * <p>\\n158:      * It is common for the assignment callback to use the consumer instance in order to query offsets. It is possible\\n159:      * for a {@link org.apache.kafka.common.errors.WakeupException} or {@link org.apache.kafka.common.errors.InterruptException}\\n160:      * to be raised from one of these nested invocations. In this case, the exception will be propagated to the current\\n161:      * invocation of {@link KafkaConsumer#poll(java.time.Duration)} in which this callback is being executed. This means it is not\\n162:      * necessary to catch these exceptions and re-attempt to wakeup or interrupt the consumer thread.\\n163:      *\\n164:      * @param partitions The list of partitions that are now assigned to the consumer (previously owned partitions will\\n165:      *                   NOT be included, i.e. this list will only include newly added partitions)\\n166:      * @throws org.apache.kafka.common.errors.WakeupException If raised from a nested call to {@link KafkaConsumer}\\n167:      * @throws org.apache.kafka.common.errors.InterruptException If raised from a nested call to {@link KafkaConsumer}\\n168:      */\\n169:     void onPartitionsAssigned(Collection<TopicPartition> partitions);\\n170: \\n171:     /**\\n172:      * A callback method you can implement to provide handling of cleaning up resources for partitions that have already\\n173:      * been reassigned to other consumers. This method will not be called during normal execution as the owned partitions would\\n174:      * first be revoked by calling the {@link ConsumerRebalanceListener#onPartitionsRevoked}, before being reassigned\\n175:      * to other consumers during a rebalance event. However, during exceptional scenarios when the consumer realized that it\\n176:      * does not own this partition any longer, i.e. not revoked via a normal rebalance event, then this method would be invoked.\\n177:      * <p>\\n178:      * For example, this function is called if a consumer's session timeout has expired, or if a fatal error has been\\n179:      * received indicating the consumer is no longer part of the group.\\n180:      * <p>\\n181:      * By default it will just trigger {@link ConsumerRebalanceListener#onPartitionsRevoked}; for users who want to distinguish\\n182:      * the handling logic of revoked partitions v.s. lost partitions, they can override the default implementation.\\n183:      * <p>\\n184:      * It is possible\\n185:      * for a {@link org.apache.kafka.common.errors.WakeupException} or {@link org.apache.kafka.common.errors.InterruptException}\\n186:      * to be raised from one of these nested invocations. In this case, the exception will be propagated to the current\\n187:      * invocation of {@link KafkaConsumer#poll(java.time.Duration)} in which this callback is being executed. This means it is not\\n188:      * necessary to catch these exceptions and re-attempt to wakeup or interrupt the consumer thread.\\n189:      *\\n190:      * @param partitions The list of partitions that were assigned to the consumer and now have been reassigned\\n191:      *                   to other consumers. With the current protocol this will always include all of the consumer's\\n192:      *                   previously assigned partitions, but this may change in future protocols (ie there would still\\n193:      *                   be some partitions left)\\n194:      * @throws org.apache.kafka.common.errors.WakeupException If raised from a nested call to {@link KafkaConsumer}\\n195:      * @throws org.apache.kafka.common.errors.InterruptException If raised from a nested call to {@link KafkaConsumer}\\n196:      */\\n197:     default void onPartitionsLost(Collection<TopicPartition> partitions) {\\n198:         onPartitionsRevoked(partitions);\\n199:     }\\n200: }\\n201: \",\"url\":\"https://sourcegraph.sourcegraph.com/github.com/sourcegraph-testing/kafka@a61b998c5df7ca9baccc5d966cb1e95108cbdee9/-/blob/clients/src/main/java/org/apache/kafka/clients/consumer/ConsumerRebalanceListener.java\"}",

This method will not be called during normal execution as the owned partitions would\n174:      * first be revoked by calling the {@link ConsumerRebalanceListener#onPartitionsRevoked}, before being reassigned\n175:      * to other consumers during a rebalance event. However, during exceptional scenarios when the consumer realized that it\n176:      * does not own this partition any longer, i.e. not revoked via a normal rebalance event, then this method would be invoked.\n177:      * <p>\n178:      * For example, this function is called if a consumer's session timeout has expired, or if a fatal error has been\n179:      * received indicating the consumer is no longer part of the group.\n180:      * <p>\n181:      * By default it will just trigger {@link ConsumerRebalanceListener#onPartitionsRevoked}; for users who want to distinguish\n182:      * the handling logic of revoked partitions v.s. lost partitions, they can override the default implementation.\n183:      * <p>\n184:      * It is possible\n185:      * for a {@link org.apache.kafka.common.errors.WakeupException} or {@link org.apache.kafka.common.errors.InterruptException}\n186:      * to be raised from one of these nested invocations. In this case, the exception will be propagated to the current\n187:      * invocation of {@link KafkaConsumer#poll(java.time.Duration)} in which this callback is being executed. This means it is not\n188:      * necessary to catch these exceptions and re-attempt to wakeup or interrupt the consumer thread.\n189:      *\n190:      * @param partitions The list of partitions that were assigned to the consumer and now have been reassigned\n191:      *                   to other consumers. With the current protocol this will always include all of the consumer's\n192:      *                   previously assigned partitions, but this may change in future protocols (ie there would still\n193:      *                   be some partitions left)\n194:      * @throws org.apache.kafka.common.errors.WakeupException If raised from a nested call to {@link KafkaConsumer}\n195:      * @throws org.apache.kafka.common.errors.InterruptException If raised from a nested call to {@link KafkaConsumer}\n196:      */\n197:     default void onPartitionsLost(Collection<TopicPartition> partitions) {\n198:         onPartitionsRevoked(partitions);\n199:     }\n200: }\n201: ",

Manual partition assignment does\\n341:  * not use group coordination, so consumer failures will not cause assigned partitions to be rebalanced. Each consumer\\n342:  * acts independently even if it shares a groupId with another consumer. To avoid offset commit conflicts, you should\\n343:  * usually ensure that the groupId is unique for each consumer instance.\\n344:  * <p>\\n345:  * Note that it isn't possible to mix manual partition assignment (i.e. using {@link #assign(Collection) assign})\\n346:  * with dynamic partition assignment through topic subscription (i.e. using {@link #subscribe(Collection) subscribe}).\\n347:  *\\n348:  * <h4><a name=\\\"rebalancecallback\\\">Storing Offsets Outside Kafka</h4>\\n349:  *\\n350:  * The consumer application need not use Kafka's built-in offset storage, it can store offsets in a store of its own\\n351:  * choosing. The primary use case for this is allowing the application to store both the offset and the results of the\\n352:  * consumption in the same system in a way that both the results and offsets are stored atomically. This is not always\\n353:  * possible, but when it is it will make the consumption fully atomic and give \\\"exactly once\\\" semantics that are\\n354:  * stronger than the default \\\"at-least once\\\" semantics you get with Kafka's offset commit functionality.\\n355:  * <p>\\n356:  * Here are a couple of examples of this type of usage:\\n357:  * <ul>\\n358:  * <li>If the results of the consumption are being stored in a relational database, storing the offset in the database\\n359:  * as well can allow committing both the results and offset in a single transaction. For\\n363:  * example a search index could be built by subscribing to a particular partition and storing both the offset and the\\n364:  * indexed data together. If this is done in a way that is atomic, it is often possible to have it be the case that even\\n365:  * if a crash occurs that causes unsync'd data to be lost, whatever is left has the corresponding offset stored as well.\\n366:  * This means that in this case the indexing process that comes back having lost recent updates just resumes indexing\\n367:  * from what it has ensuring that no updates are lost.\\n368:  * </ul>\\n369:  * <p>\\n370:  * Each record comes with its own offset, so to manage your own offset you just need to do the following:\\n371:  *\\n372:  * <ul>\\n373:  * <li>Configure <code>enable.auto.commit=false</code>\\n374:  * <li>Use the offset provided with each {@link ConsumerRecord} to save your position.\\n375:  * <li>On restart restore the position of the consumer using {@link #seek(TopicPartition, long)}.\\n376:  * </ul>\\n377:  *\\n378:  * <p>\\n379:  * This type of usage is simplest when the partition assignment is also done manually (this would be likely in the\\n380:  * search index use case described above). This can be done by providing a\\n382:  * {@link ConsumerRebalanceListener} instance in the call to {@link #subscribe(Collection, ConsumerRebalanceListener)}\\n383:  * and {@link #subscribe(Pattern, ConsumerRebalanceListener)}.\\n384:  * For example, when partitions are taken from a consumer the consumer will want to commit its offset for those partitions by\\n385:  * implementing {@link ConsumerRebalanceListener#onPartitionsRevoked(Collection)}. However Kafka allows the consumer to manually control its position,\\n396:  * moving forward or backwards in a partition at will. This means a consumer can re-consume older records, or skip to\\n397:  * the most recent records without actually consuming the intermediate records.\\n398:  * <p>\\n399:  * There are several instances where manually controlling the consumer's position can be useful.\\n400:  * <p>\\n401:  * One case is for time-sensitive record processing it may make sense for a consumer that falls far enough behind to not\\n402:  * attempt to catch up processing all records, but rather just skip to the most recent records.\\n403:  * <p>\\n404:  * Another use case is for a system that maintains local state as described in the previous section. In such a system\\n405:  * the consumer will want to initialize its position on start-up to whatever is contained in the local store. Likewise\\n406:  * if the local state is destroyed (say because the disk is lost) the state may be recreated on a new machine by\\n407:  * re-consuming all the data and recreating the state (assuming that Kafka is retaining sufficient history).\\n408:  * <p>\\n409:  * Kafka allows specifying the position using {@link #seek(TopicPartition, long)} to specify the new position. Special\\n410:  * methods for seeking to the earliest and latest offset the server maintains are also available (\\n411:  * {@link #seekToBeginning(Collection)} and {@link #seekToEnd(Collection)} respectively).\\n412:  *\\n413:  * <h4>Consumption Flow Control</h4>\\n414:  *\\n415:  * If a consumer is assigned multiple partitions to fetch data from, it will try to consume from all of them at the same time,\\n416:  * effectively giving these partitions the same priority for consumption. However in some cases consumers may want to\\n417:  * first focus on fetching from some subset of the assigned partitions at full speed, and only start fetching other partitions\\n418:  * when these partitions have few or no data to consume.\\n419:  *\\n420:  * <p>\\n421:  * One of such cases is stream processing, where processor fetches from two topics and performs the join on these two streams.\\n422:  * When one of the topics is long lagging behind the other, the processor would like to pause fetching from the ahead topic\\n423:  * in order to get the lagging stream to catch up. Another example is bootstraping upon consumer starting up where there are\\n424:  * a lot of history data to catch up, the applications usually want to get the latest data on some of the topics before consider\\n425:  * fetching other topics.\\n426:  *\\n427:  * <p>\\n428:  * Kafka supports dynamic controlling of consumption flows by using {@link #pause(Collection)} and {@link #resume(Collection)}\\n429:  * to pause the consumption on the specified assigned partitions and resume the consumption\\n430:  * on the specified paused partitions respectively in the future {@link #poll(Duration)} calls.\\n431:  *\\n432:  * <h3>Reading Transactional Messages</h3>\\n433:  *\\n434:  * <p>\\n435:  * Transactions were introduced in Kafka 0.11.0 wherein applications can write to multiple topics and partitions atomically.\\n436:  * In order for this to work, consumers reading from these partitions should be configured to only read committed data.\\n437:  * This can be achieved by setting the {@code isolation.level=read_committed} in the consumer's configuration.\\n438:  *\\n439:  * <p>\\n440:  * In <code>read_committed</code> mode, the consumer will read only those transactional messages which have been\\n441:  * successfully committed. It will continue to read non-transactional messages as before. There is no client-side\\n442:  * buffering in <code>read_committed</code> mode. Instead, the end offset of a partition for a <code>read_committed</code>\\n443:  * consumer would be the offset of the first message in the partition belonging to an open transaction. This offset\\n444:  * is known as the 'Last Stable Offset'(LSO).</p>\\n445:  *\\n446:  * <p>\\n447:  * A {@code read_committed} consumer will only read up to the LSO and filter out any transactional\\n448:  * messages which have been aborted. The LSO also affects the behavior of {@link #seekToEnd(Collection)} and\\n449:  * {@link #endOffsets(Collection)} for {@code read_committed} consumers, details of which are in each method's documentation.\\n450:  * Finally, the fetch lag metrics are also adjusted to be relative to the LSO for {@code read_committed} consumers.\\n451:  *\\n452:  * <p>\\n453:  * Partitions with transactional messages will include commit or abort markers which indicate the result of a transaction.\\n454:  * There markers are not returned to applications, yet have an offset in the log. As a result, applications reading from\\n455:  * topics with transactional messages will see gaps in the consumed offsets. These missing messages would be the transaction\\n456:  * markers, and they are filtered out for consumers in both isolation levels. Additionally, applications using\\n457:  * {@code read_committed} consumers may also see gaps due to aborted transactions, since those messages would not\\n458:  * be returned by the consumer and yet would have valid offsets.\\n459:  *\\n460:  * <h3><a name=\\\"multithreaded\\\">Multi-threaded Processing</a></h3>\\n461:  *\\n462:  * The Kafka consumer is NOT thread-safe. All network I/O happens in the thread of the application\\n463:  * making the call. It is the responsibility of the user to ensure that multi-threaded access\\n464:  * is properly synchronized. Un-synchronized access will result in {@link ConcurrentModificationException}.\\n465:  *\\n466:  * <p>\\n467:  * The only exception to this rule is {@link #wakeup()}, which can safely be used from an external thread to\\n468:  * interrupt an active operation. In this case, a {@link org.apache.kafka.common.errors.WakeupException} will be\\n469:  * thrown from the thread blocking on the operation. This can be used to shutdown the consumer from another thread.\\n470:  * The following snippet shows the typical pattern:\\n471:  *\\n472:  * <pre>\\n473:  * public class KafkaConsumerRunner implements Runnable {\\n474:  *     private final AtomicBoolean closed = new AtomicBoolean(false);\\n475:  *     private final KafkaConsumer consumer;\\n476:  *\\n477:  *     public KafkaConsumerRunner(KafkaConsumer consumer) {\\n478:  *       this.consumer = consumer;\\n479:  *     }\\n480:  *\\n481:  *     {@literal}@Override\\n482:  *     public void run() {\\n483:  *         try {\\n484:  *             consumer.subscribe(Arrays.asList(\\\"topic\\\"));\\n485:  *             while (!closed.get()) {\\n486:  *                 ConsumerRecords records = consumer.poll(Duration.ofMillis(10000));\\n487:  *                 // Handle new records\\n488:  *             }\\n489:  *         } catch (WakeupException e) {\\n490:  *             // Ignore exception if closing\\n491:  *             if (!closed.get()) throw e;\\n492:  *         } finally {\\n493:  *             consumer.close();\\n494:  *         }\\n495:  *     }\\n496:  *\\n497:  *     // Shutdown hook which can be called from a separate thread\\n498:  *     public void shutdown() {\\n499:  *         closed.set(true);\\n500:  *         consumer.wakeup();\\n501:  *     }\\n502:  * }\\n503:  * </pre>\\n504:  *\\n505:  * Then in a separate thread, the consumer can be shutdown by setting the closed flag and waking up the consumer.\\n506:  *\\n507:  * <p>\\n508:  * <pre>\\n509:  *     closed.set(true);\\n510:  *     consumer.wakeup();\\n511:  * </pre>\\n512:  *\\n513:  * <p>\\n514:  * Note that while it is possible to use thread interrupts instead of {@link #wakeup()} to abort a blocking operation\\n515:  * (in which case, {@link InterruptException} will be raised), we discourage their use since they may cause a clean\\n516:  * shutdown of the consumer to be aborted. Interrupts are mainly supported for those cases where using {@link #wakeup()}\\n517:  * is impossible, e.g. when a consumer thread is managed by code that is unaware of the Kafka client.\\n518:  *\\n519:  * <p>\\n520:  * We have intentionally avoided implementing a particular threading model for processing. This leaves several\\n521:  * options for implementing multi-threaded processing of records.\\n522:  *\\n523:  * <h4>1. One Consumer Per Thread</h4>\\n524:  *\\n525:  * A simple option is to give each thread its own consumer instance. Here are the pros and cons of this approach:\\n526:  * <ul>\\n527:  * <li><b>PRO</b>: It is the easiest to implement\\n528:  * <li><b>PRO</b>: It is often the fastest as no inter-thread co-ordination is needed\\n529:  * <li><b>PRO</b>: It makes in-order processing on a per-partition basis very easy to implement (each thread just\\n530:  * processes messages in the order it receives them).\\n531:  * <li><b>CON</b>: More consumers means more TCP connections to the cluster (one per thread). In general Kafka handles\\n532:  * connections very efficiently so this is generally a small cost.\\n533:  * <li><b>CON</b>: Multiple consumers means more requests being sent to the server and slightly less batching of data\\n534:  * which can cause some drop in I/O throughput.\\n535:  * <li><b>CON</b>: The number of total threads across all processes will be limited by the total number of partitions.\\n536:  * </ul>\\n537:  *\\n538:  * <h4>2. For processing that has no ordering requirements this is not a problem.\\n551:  * <li><b>CON</b>: Manually committing the position becomes harder as it requires that all threads co-ordinate to ensure\\n552:  * that processing is complete for that partition.\\n553:  * </ul>\\n554:  *\\n555:  * There are many possible variations on this approach. This is not always\n353:  * possible, but when it is it will make the consumption fully atomic and give \"exactly once\" semantics that are\n354:  * stronger than the default \"at-least once\" semantics you get with Kafka's offset commit functionality.\n355:  * <p>\n356:  * Here are a couple of examples of this type of usage:\n357:  * <ul>\n358:  * <li>If the results of the consumption are being stored in a relational database, storing the offset in the database\n359:  * as well can allow committing both the results and offset in a single transaction. Thus either the transaction will\n360:  * succeed and the offset will be updated based on what was consumed or the result will not be stored and the offset\n361:  * won't be updated.\n362:  * <li>If the results are being stored in a local store it may be possible to store the offset there as well. For\n363:  * example a search index could be built by subscribing to a particular partition and storing both the offset and the\n364:  * indexed data together. If this is done in a way that is atomic, it is often possible to have it be the case that even\n365:  * if a crash occurs that causes unsync'd data to be lost, whatever is left has the corresponding offset stored as well.\n366:  * This means that in this case the indexing process that comes back having lost recent updates just resumes indexing\n367:  * from what it has ensuring that no updates are lost.\n368:  * </ul>\n369:  * <p>\n370:  * Each record comes with its own offset, so to manage your own offset you just need to do the following:\n371:  *\n372:  * <ul>\n373:  * <li>Configure <code>enable.auto.commit=false</code>\n374:  * <li>Use the offset provided with each {@link ConsumerRecord} to save your position.\n375:  * <li>On restart restore the position of the consumer using {@link #seek(TopicPartition, long)}.\n376:  * </ul>\n377:  *\n378:  * <p>\n379:  * This type of usage is simplest when the partition assignment is also done manually (this would be likely in the\n380:  * search index use case described above). If the partition assignment is done automatically special care is\n381:  * needed to handle the case where partition assignments change. This can be done by providing a\n382:  * {@link ConsumerRebalanceListener} instance in the call to {@link #subscribe(Collection, ConsumerRebalanceListener)}\n383:  * and {@link #subscribe(Pattern, ConsumerRebalanceListener)}.\n384:  * For example, when partitions are taken from a consumer the consumer will want to commit its offset for those partitions by\n385:  * implementing {@link ConsumerRebalanceListener#onPartitionsRevoked(Collection)}. When partitions are assigned to a\n386:  * consumer, the consumer will want to look up the offset for those new partitions and correctly initialize the consumer\n387:  * to that position by implementing {@link ConsumerRebalanceListener#onPartitionsAssigned(Collection)}.\n388:  * <p>\n389:  * Another common use for {@link ConsumerRebalanceListener} is to flush any caches the application maintains for\n390:  * partitions that are moved elsewhere.\n391:  *\n392:  * <h4>Controlling The Consumer's Position</h4>\n393:  *\n394:  * In most use cases the consumer will simply consume records from beginning to end, periodically committing its\n395:  * position (either automatically or manually). However Kafka allows the consumer to manually control its position,\n396:  * moving forward or backwards in a partition at will. This means a consumer can re-consume older records, or skip to\n397:  * the most recent records without actually consuming the intermediate records.\n398:  * <p>\n399:  * There are several instances where manually controlling the consumer's position can be useful.\n400:  * <p>\n401:  * One case is for time-sensitive record processing it may make sense for a consumer that falls far enough behind to not\n402:  * attempt to catch up processing all records, but rather just skip to the most recent records.\n403:  * <p>\n404:  * Another use case is for a system that maintains local state as described in the previous section. In such a system\n405:  * the consumer will want to initialize its position on start-up to whatever is contained in the local store. Likewise\n406:  * if the local state is destroyed (say because the disk is lost) the state may be recreated on a new machine by\n407:  * re-consuming all the data and recreating the state (assuming that Kafka is retaining sufficient history).\n408:  * <p>\n409:  * Kafka allows specifying the position using {@link #seek(TopicPartition, long)} to specify the new position. Special\n410:  * methods for seeking to the earliest and latest offset the server maintains are also available (\n411:  * {@link #seekToBeginning(Collection)} and {@link #seekToEnd(Collection)} respectively).\n412:  *\n413:  * <h4>Consumption Flow Control</h4>\n414:  *\n415:  * If a consumer is assigned multiple partitions to fetch data from, it will try to consume from all of them at the same time,\n416:  * effectively giving these partitions the same priority for consumption. However in some cases consumers may want to\n417:  * first focus on fetching from some subset of the assigned partitions at full speed, and only start fetching other partitions\n418:  * when these partitions have few or no data to consume.\n419:  *\n420:  * <p>\n421:  * One of such cases is stream processing, where processor fetches from two topics and performs the join on these two streams.\n422:  * When one of the topics is long lagging behind the other, the processor would like to pause fetching from the ahead topic\n423:  * in order to get the lagging stream to catch up. Another example is bootstraping upon consumer starting up where there are\n424:  * a lot of history data to catch up, the applications usually want to get the latest data on some of the topics before consider\n425:  * fetching other topics.\n426:  *\n427:  * <p>\n428:  * Kafka supports dynamic controlling of consumption flows by using {@link #pause(Collection)} and {@link #resume(Collection)}\n429:  * to pause the consumption on the specified assigned partitions and resume the consumption\n430:  * on the specified paused partitions respectively in the future {@link #poll(Duration)} calls.\n431:  *\n432:  * <h3>Reading Transactional Messages</h3>\n433:  *\n434:  * <p>\n435:  * Transactions were introduced in Kafka 0.11.0 wherein applications can write to multiple topics and partitions atomically.\n436:  * In order for this to work, consumers reading from these partitions should be configured to only read committed data.\n437:  * This can be achieved by setting the {@code isolation.level=read_committed} in the consumer's configuration.\n438:  *\n439:  * <p>\n440:  * In <code>read_committed</code> mode, the consumer will read only those transactional messages which have been\n441:  * successfully committed. It will continue to read non-transactional messages as before. There is no client-side\n442:  * buffering in <code>read_committed</code> mode. Instead, the end offset of a partition for a <code>read_committed</code>\n443:  * consumer would be the offset of the first message in the partition belonging to an open transaction. This offset\n444:  * is known as the 'Last Stable Offset'(LSO).</p>\n445:  *\n446:  * <p>\n447:  * A {@code read_committed} consumer will only read up to the LSO and filter out any transactional\n448:  * messages which have been aborted. The LSO also affects the behavior of {@link #seekToEnd(Collection)} and\n449:  * {@link #endOffsets(Collection)} for {@code read_committed} consumers, details of which are in each method's documentation.\n450:  * Finally, the fetch lag metrics are also adjusted to be relative to the LSO for {@code read_committed} consumers.\n451:  *\n452:  * <p>\n453:  * Partitions with transactional messages will include commit or abort markers which indicate the result of a transaction.\n454:  * There markers are not returned to applications, yet have an offset in the log. As a result, applications reading from\n455:  * topics with transactional messages will see gaps in the consumed offsets. These missing messages would be the transaction\n456:  * markers, and they are filtered out for consumers in both isolation levels. Additionally, applications using\n457:  * {@code read_committed} consumers may also see gaps due to aborted transactions, since those messages would not\n458:  * be returned by the consumer and yet would have valid offsets.\n459:  *\n460:  * <h3><a name=\"multithreaded\">Multi-threaded Processing</a></h3>\n461:  *\n462:  * The Kafka consumer is NOT thread-safe. All network I/O happens in the thread of the application\n463:  * making the call. It is the responsibility of the user to ensure that multi-threaded access\n464:  * is properly synchronized. Un-synchronized access will result in {@link ConcurrentModificationException}.\n465:  *\n466:  * <p>\n467:  * The only exception to this rule is {@link #wakeup()}, which can safely be used from an external thread to\n468:  * interrupt an active operation. In this case, a {@link org.apache.kafka.common.errors.WakeupException} will be\n469:  * thrown from the thread blocking on the operation. This can be used to shutdown the consumer from another thread.\n470:  * The following snippet shows the typical pattern:\n471:  *\n472:  * <pre>\n473:  * public class KafkaConsumerRunner implements Runnable {\n474:  *     private final AtomicBoolean closed = new AtomicBoolean(false);\n475:  *     private final KafkaConsumer consumer;\n476:  *\n477:  *     public KafkaConsumerRunner(KafkaConsumer consumer) {\n478:  *       this.consumer = consumer;\n479:  *     }\n480:  *\n481:  *     {@literal}@Override\n482:  *     public void run() {\n483:  *         try {\n484:  *             consumer.subscribe(Arrays.asList(\"topic\"));\n485:  *             while (!closed.get()) {\n486:  *                 ConsumerRecords records = consumer.poll(Duration.ofMillis(10000));\n487:  *                 // Handle new records\n488:  *             }\n489:  *         } catch (WakeupException e) {\n490:  *             // Ignore exception if closing\n491:  *             if (!closed.get()) throw e;\n492:  *         } finally {\n493:  *             consumer.close();\n494:  *         }\n495:  *     }\n496:  *\n497:  *     // Shutdown hook which can be called from a separate thread\n498:  *     public void shutdown() {\n499:  *         closed.set(true);\n500:  *         consumer.wakeup();\n501:  *     }\n502:  * }\n503:  * </pre>\n504:  *\n505:  * Then in a separate thread, the consumer can be shutdown by setting the closed flag and waking up the consumer.\n506:  *\n507:  * <p>\n508:  * <pre>\n509:  *     closed.set(true);\n510:  *     consumer.wakeup();\n511:  * </pre>\n512:  *\n513:  * <p>\n514:  * Note that while it is possible to use thread interrupts instead of {@link #wakeup()} to abort a blocking operation\n515:  * (in which case, {@link InterruptException} will be raised), we discourage their use since they may cause a clean\n516:  * shutdown of the consumer to be aborted. Interrupts are mainly supported for those cases where using {@link #wakeup()}\n517:  * is impossible, e.g. when a consumer thread is managed by code that is unaware of the Kafka client.\n518:  *\n519:  * <p>\n520:  * We have intentionally avoided implementing a particular threading model for processing. This leaves several\n521:  * options for implementing multi-threaded processing of records.\n522:  *\n523:  * <h4>1. One Consumer Per Thread</h4>\n524:  *\n525:  * A simple option is to give each thread its own consumer instance. Here are the pros and cons of this approach:\n526:  * <ul>\n527:  * <li><b>PRO</b>: It is the easiest to implement\n528:  * <li><b>PRO</b>: It is often the fastest as no inter-thread co-ordination is needed\n529:  * <li><b>PRO</b>: It makes in-order processing on a per-partition basis very easy to implement (each thread just\n530:  * processes messages in the order it receives them).\n531:  * <li><b>CON</b>: More consumers means more TCP connections to the cluster (one per thread). In general Kafka handles\n532:  * connections very efficiently so this is generally a small cost.\n533:  * <li><b>CON</b>: Multiple consumers means more requests being sent to the server and slightly less batching of data\n534:  * which can cause some drop in I/O throughput.\n535:  * <li><b>CON</b>: The number of total threads across all processes will be limited by the total number of partitions.\n536:  * </ul>\n537:  *\n538:  * <h4>2. Decouple Consumption and Processing</h4>\n539:  *\n540:  * Another alternative is to have one or more consumer threads that do all data consumption and hands off\n541:  * {@link ConsumerRecords} instances to a blocking queue consumed by a pool of processor threads that actually handle\n542:  * the record processing.\n543:  *\n544:  * This option likewise has pros and cons:\n545:  * <ul>\n546:  * <li><b>PRO</b>: This option allows independently scaling the number of consumers and processors. This makes it\n547:  * possible to have a single consumer that feeds many processor threads, avoiding any limitation on partitions.\n548:  * <li><b>CON</b>: Guaranteeing order across the processors requires particular care as the threads will execute\n549:  * independently an earlier chunk of data may actually be processed after a later chunk of data just due to the luck of\n550:  * thread execution timing. For processing that has no ordering requirements this is not a problem.\n551:  * <li><b>CON</b>: Manually committing the position becomes harder as it requires that all threads co-ordinate to ensure\n552:  * that processing is complete for that partition.\n553:  * </ul>\n554:  *\n555:  * There are many possible variations on this approach. For example each processor thread can have its own queue, and\n556:  * the consumer threads can hash into these queues using the TopicPartition to ensure in-order consumption and simplify\n557:  * commit.\n558:  */\n559: public class KafkaConsumer<K, V> implements Consumer<K, V> {\n560: \n561:     private static final String CLIENT_ID_METRIC_TAG = \"client-id\";\n562:     private static final
